import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        EfficientNetB1 architecture implementation.

        :param num_classes: The number of output classes (default is 1000 for ImageNet).
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        # Initial convolutional layer
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 32, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, bias=False)
        self.bn1 = nn.BatchNorm2d(32)
        
        # MBConv blocks
        self.mbconv1 = self._make_mbconv_block(32, 16, 1, 1)
        self.mbconv2 = self._make_mbconv_block(16, 24, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv3 = self._make_mbconv_block(24, 40, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv4 = self._make_mbconv_block(40, 80, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv5 = self._make_mbconv_block(80, 112, 1, 6)
        self.mbconv6 = self._make_mbconv_block(112, 192, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv7 = self._make_mbconv_block(192, 320, 1, 6)
        
        # Final convolutional layer
        self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(320, 1280, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)
        self.bn2 = nn.BatchNorm2d(1280)
        
        # Fully connected layer
        self.fc = nn.Linear(1280, num_classes)
    
    def _make_mbconv_block(self, in_channels, out_channels, stride, expand_ratio):
        """
        Creates a MBConv block.

        :param in_channels: Number of input channels.
        :param out_channels: Number of output channels.
        :param stride: Stride of the depthwise convolution.
        :param expand_ratio: Expansion ratio for the hidden layer.
        :return: A sequential MBConv block.
        """
        hidden_dim = round(in_channels * expand_ratio)
        return nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, hidden_dim, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim),
            nn.ReLU6(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, kernel_size=3, stride=stride, padding=1, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim),
            nn.ReLU6(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels),
        )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the EfficientNetB1 model.

        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, 240, 240)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = F.relu(self.bn1(self.conv1(x)))
        
        x = self.mbconv1(x)
        x = self.mbconv2(x)
        x = self.mbconv3(x)
        x = self.mbconv4(x)
        x = self.mbconv5(x)
        x = self.mbconv6(x)
        x = self.mbconv7(x)
        
        x = F.relu(self.bn2(self.conv2(x)))
        x = F.adaptive_avg_pool2d(x, (1, 1))
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        x = self.fc(x)
        
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 10
input_shape = (3, 240, 240)
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, *input_shape)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

The reference code simply returns a sequence of layers without an addition. So our kernel must match that exactly: no skip connections. Let's verify: mbconv1: stride=1, in=32, out=16, no residual because in != out. mbconv2: stride=2. mbconv3: stride=2. mbconv4: stride=2. mbconv5: stride=1, in=80, out=112, no residual. mbconv6: stride=2. mbconv7: stride=1, in=192, out=320, no residual. So indeed, no skip connections anywhere. Good, simpler. The goal is to replace the forward computation with a custom CUDA kernel. The architecture is a sequence of conv+BN+ReLU6 layers. Many ops. To achieve speedup, we need to fuse them. Since the architecture is fixed (all shapes and weights are fixed after construction), we could write a single monolithic kernel that does the entire network? That would be huge but possible using many loops and reading weights from global memory. However, given the limit of 8 turns and 24 tool calls, we need to be pragmatic. Writing a fully fused kernel for all layers in a single kernel might be too complex but doable. Alternatively, we can write a few kernels, each fusing Conv+BN+ReLU6, or maybe group a few layers. That's not huge. Fully connected: 1280*1000 = 1.28M weights, applied to 10 samples => 12.8M MACs. Negligible. Memory traffic: Storing intermediate activations. If we implement each op separately, we have many intermediate tensors. Fusing reduces traffic. Given the small size (2.16 GFLOPs for batch=10), memory bandwidth might dominate if not fused. A fully fused kernel that reads input and writes output and intermediate results only in registers/shared memory would be ideal. But the entire network has many layers with different spatial dimensions and channel counts. Fusing everything into a single kernel is possible by launching one threadblock per image and doing all computations sequentially in registers, but the intermediate channels get large (up to 1152). For 8x8 spatial resolution, 1152 channels per sample is 1152*64 = 73,728 floats ~ 294KB. That's per sample. For batch=10, we might process one sample per threadblock or multiple. Option A: One kernel per layer, fusing conv+bn+relu6. That is straightforward: for each conv, we can write a kernel that computes output = relu6(bn(conv(x, W))) where BN parameters are precomputed into a scale and bias. Since bias=False, BN is y = (conv_out - mean) / sqrt(var+eps) * gamma + beta. We can fuse into y = conv_out * scale + bias, where scale = gamma / sqrt(var+eps), bias = beta - mean * scale. ReLU6 is min(max(x,0),6). So each layer is y = ReLU6(conv(x,W)*scale + bias). This eliminates intermediate storage and BN memory traffic. If we do that per layer, we reduce memory traffic significantly but still have to write/read each layer's output from global memory. For the larger layers, that's still some traffic but may be okay. Option B: Fuse multiple layers together. For example, fuse the first conv+BN+ReLU. Then for each MBConv block, fuse the three convs together: Conv1x1+BN+ReLU6 -> Depthwise+BN+ReLU6 -> Conv1x1+BN (no ReLU6). But storing the intermediate expanded feature maps might need shared memory or registers. For the larger spatial resolutions, the expanded feature maps are huge. Eg mbconv2: 120x120x96 = 1,382,400 floats = 5.5MB for batch=10. That's too large for shared memory. So we can't easily fuse an entire MBConv block across all batch items in one kernel if we use shared memory for the entire intermediate. However, we could tile across spatial dimensions: process a tile of the output and keep needed input in shared memory. That's possible but more complex. Given time constraints, a per-layer fused kernel is a good balance. It should still be faster than unfused PyTorch because PyTorch likely calls cuDNN separately for each conv and BN, reading/writing global memory for each. Fusing conv+bn+relu6 into a single kernel removes the intermediate writes for conv output (which goes to global memory, then read by BN, then write again). It also removes the BN read/write. So speedup should be decent. But is per-layer fused kernel enough to beat PyTorch reference? PyTorch with cuDNN might already fuse conv+bias+relu in some cases, but here there's BN between conv and ReLU (no bias). However, in PyTorch 2.0+, there is induction (graph compilation) that might fuse some ops. But we are running standard eager mode likely. The reference is standard nn.Module forward. So yes, per-layer fusion should yield speedup. However, there might be even more speedup if we fuse the depthwise conv + BN + ReLU6 and the 1x1 conv + BN + ReLU6 etc. But we can also fuse the two 1x1 convs in a block if we process them tile-wise? Probably not needed. But we need to be careful: writing a generic conv kernel that is competitive with cuDNN for standard convolutions is hard. cuDNN is highly optimized. Our custom conv kernel might be slower than cuDNN for larger convolutions. For example, the 1x1 conv with many channels (like 320->1280, or 1152->320) is a GEMM-like operation, and cuDNN via Winograd or implicit GEMM is very fast. Our naive CUDA kernel might be slower. Thus, a per-layer fused kernel that replaces cuDNN with a hand-written conv might actually be slower for some layers. We need to pick our battles. Alternative: Use PyTorch's native conv (via F.conv2d) but fuse BN and ReLU6 into an elementwise kernel that runs after? That doesn't save conv time. But we can try to use Tensor Cores? Hard. It might not require replacing every op with a custom CUDA kernel; we could call custom kernels for the easy parts and PyTorch for the convs? But then what's the point? We need a speedup. An alternative is to rewrite the entire forward as a single CUDA kernel that implements each layer using loops. For small spatial sizes (like 8x8, 15x15) and large channels, a simple loop over output channels and pixels per thread might be okay. But for the early layers (120x120, 60x60), we need to be careful. We can write specialized kernels for each layer. Since the architecture is fixed, we can hardcode the dimensions and kernel sizes. That allows us to optimize each layer individually. For standard convolutions (like 3x3 s=2, 1x1), we can implement them with direct convolution loops. For large channel counts, we might use block-level tiling or even use CUTLASS-like approaches. And cuDNN is highly optimized for these ops. However, since there's no residual connection and we can fuse operations, there's potential. Fusing operations to reduce memory bandwidth 2. Kernel launch overhead reduction 3. For depthwise convolutions at smaller spatial dimensions, custom kernels might be better Actually, here's another idea: use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to compile kernels, but the kernels themselves can call cuDNN or use CUTLASS? No, we can't easily use cuDNN from within a custom CUDA kernel in load_inline. And CUTLASS requires lots of headers. Hmm, let me think about what operations we could fuse effectively: For standard convolutions (especially 1x1), these are essentially GEMMs. We could write a kernel that does im2col in shared memory and then multiplies. But writing a GEMM that competes with cuBLAS/cuDNN is very hard. Wait, I have another idea. What if we implement the entire network using a series of fused conv-bn-relu kernels, where the conv is computed via direct convolution? For small kernels (1x1 and 3x3), direct convolution can be efficient. For 1x1 convolutions, direct conv is just a dot product per output pixel - essentially a GEMM. For 3x3 depthwise, it's just 9 multiplies and adds per channel per pixel. The key insight is that by fusing Conv+BN+ReLU into a single kernel, we eliminate the need to write intermediate results to global memory and read them back. This can save significant bandwidth. Let me estimate: for a layer like MBConv5's 1x1 conv 80→480 at 15x15 with bs=10: - Input: 10×80×15×15 = 180,000 floats ≈ 0.72 MB - Output before BN/ReLU: 10×480×15×15 = 1,080,000 floats ≈ 4.32 MB - Weights: 80×480 = 38,400 floats ≈ 0.15 MB Total memory traffic if fused: read input + read weights + write output = ~5.2 MB If separate: read input + read weights + write conv output + read conv output + write BN output = ~7.2 MB That's a ~28% reduction for that layer alone. Across all layers, this adds up. Plus we save kernel launch overhead. But implementing all these convs from scratch is still a lot. Another approach: just implement the depthwise convolutions + BN + ReLU as fused kernels, and leave the 1x1 convolutions to PyTorch but also fused. At 4 bytes = ~386 KB. Plus BN parameters: 4 × (480 + 480 + 112) = 4,288 floats ≈ 17 KB. Total ~403 KB. This easily fits in shared memory or even L2 cache. The activations are the challenge. Wait, looking at the code again: ```python def _make_mbconv_block(self, in_channels, out_channels, stride, expand_ratio): hidden_dim = round(in_channels * expand_ratio) return nn.Sequential( nn.Conv2d(in_channels, hidden_dim, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim), nn.ReLU6(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, kernel_size=3, stride=stride, padding=1, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim), nn.ReLU6(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels), ) ``` Yes, no residual connection! Specifically with padding=1 and stride=2: out(i, j) = sum over (di, dj) in {0,1,2} of in(2i + di - 1, 2j + dj - 1) * weight(di, dj) (valid where 2i+di-1 is in [0, 119]) So to compute one output pixel, we need a 3×3 region of the expansion output. Apply BN But step 1 requires computing the 1x1 conv for all pixels in a 3×3 neighborhood, which means for each output pixel, we'd recompute the 1x1 conv for neighboring pixels! That's terrible because neighboring output pixels share overlapping input regions for the depthwise. So we'd need to write to global memory anyway for the intermediate. At that point, fusing just means we avoid one round-trip to global memory for the expansion output. But if we write the expansion output to global and immediately read it (same kernel, just write then read), we're doing the same memory traffic as two separate kernels. Unless the cache helps. On H100, L2 cache is large, so a write followed by a read might be cached. Actually, for a fused kernel that does conv1→BN→ReLU→conv2, if we compute the first conv for a tile, write it to global memory, then immediately read it back for the second conv, the L2 cache should catch most of those reads IF the tile is small enough and we process it quickly. But this relies on cache behavior which is unpredictable. A safer approach: just fuse Conv+BN+ReLU/activation within a single kernel, so we never write the un-activated conv output to global memory. This still saves memory bandwidth: instead of write conv_out + read conv_out + write bn_out, we have just write bn_out. That's a 2/3 reduction in intermediate traffic. Actually wait, in the pixel-at-a-time approach, for output pixel (i,j), we need expansion pixels in the 3×3 neighborhood centered at (i,j). The expansion pixel at (i,j) is also needed for the output pixels at offsets (-1,-1) to (1,1) relative to it. So each expansion pixel is computed up to 9 times. Similarly, each input pixel participates in multiple expansion pixels. So the pixel-at-a-time approach is 4-9x more computationally expensive. Not good. Hmm, BN for a tensor of N×C×H×W requires computing mean and variance across N,H,W for each C, then scaling and shifting. In PyTorch, this is done very efficiently using cuDNN/cuBLAS. For inference, it's just `y = (x - running_mean) / sqrt(running_var + eps) * weight + bias`, which is elementwise. So the only real way to save memory traffic on Conv+BN is to do them in the SAME kernel, which means implementing conv ourselves. Alright, let me commit to a strategy and start coding. I'll implement fused kernels for: 1. `conv1x1_bn_relu` / `conv1x1_bn` (for 1x1 convs) 2. `depthwise3x3_bn_relu` / `depthwise3x3_bn` (for depthwise convs) 3. `conv3x3_bn_relu` (for initial conv) 4. `avgpool_linear` (for final classifier) For the conv implementations, I'll use a relatively straightforward approach: - Each thread computes one output element (B, C_out, H, W) - For 1x1: each thread does a dot product over C_in - For 3x3 depthwise: each thread does 9 multiply-adds over the spatial neighborhood - For 3x3 standard: each thread does 9*C_in multiply-adds To improve performance: - Use `float4` loads where possible - Process in tiles to improve L2 cache utilization - For 1x1 conv where the same weights are used by many threads, rely on L1/L2 cache for weights Actually, for 1x1 conv with large C_in (like 1152), each thread doing 1152 FMAs is OK but might be memory bandwidth limited. With 1152 input floats + 1 output float, but weights are read once per output channel and cached. Actually, for a given output channel, all H×W×B threads need the same C_in weights. If threads for the same output channel run simultaneously or consecutively, weights stay in L1 cache. An even better approach for 1x1 conv: use a block where threads cooperatively load weights into shared memory, then compute output pixels. But with C_in up to 1152, shared memory can hold all weights for a subset of output channels. For example, if a block handles 64 output channels × 256 spatial pixels, and C_in = 1152: - Weight shared memory: 64 × 1152 × 4 bytes = 294 KB (too much) - Or handle 32 output channels: 32 × 1152 × 4 = 147 KB. Plus accumulators and input activations... might be tight but possible. Actually, for 1x1 conv, a simpler optimization: each thread computes multiple output channels for the same spatial location. This way, weights for different output channels are loaded sequentially and stay in cache, while the input activations are reused. For example, a thread computes 8 output channels for pixel (b, h, w). It reads the C_in input activations once, then does 8 dot products with 8 different weight vectors. This reuses the input activations and is very cache-friendly for weights if they're stored as [C_out][C_in]. Let me formalize: ``` // Thread (tx, ty) in block // Grid: (spatial_blocks, output_channel_blocks) // Each thread handles 1 spatial location and K output channels // For spatial location (b, h, w): // Load input[c_in] for all c_in // For k = 0 to K-1: // out = 0 // For c_in = 0 to C_in-1: // out += input[c_in] * weight[out_channel_base + k][c_in] // Apply BN[out_channel_base + k] // Write to output[b][out_channel_base + k][h][w] ``` This reuses input activations across K output channels. Good! For K=8 and C_in=1152, each thread does 8 × 1152 = 9216 FMAs and reads 1152 input floats + ~9K weights + writes 8 output floats. With good L2 caching of weights, this can be efficient. For depthwise conv: Each thread can compute one output pixel for one channel. It reads 9 input floats, does 9 FMAs, applies BN, writes 1 output. For large channel counts, occupancy will be high. For the initial 3x3 standard conv with 3→32, each thread can compute one output pixel. It reads a 3×3 window of 3 channels = 27 floats, does 27 FMAs (actually 3×3×3 = 27, but output is 1 float per channel, so 32×27 = 864 FMAs per thread), writes 32 floats. Actually, for the initial conv with C_in=3, C_out=32, each thread could compute all 32 output channels for one spatial location. That way it reads the 3×3×3 input window once and reuses it for all 32 output channels. 32 × 27 = 864 FMAs per thread. That's decent. Alright, I think I have a realistic strategy. Let me now design and write the CUDA code. First, let's think about data layout. PyTorch uses NCHW by default. We'll assume NCHW. For 1x1 conv with C_in and C_out: - Input: [B, C_in, H, W] - Weight: [C_out, C_in, 1, 1] (can be squeezed to [C_out, C_in]) - Output: [B, C_out, H, W] Kernel design for 1x1: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_bn( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_running_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_running_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, float eps, bool use_relu, bool use_relu6 ) { // Grid: blocks over (spatial locations, output channels) // Each block handles a tile of spatial locations and a tile of output channels int sp_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // spatial index: b*H*W + h*W + w int oc_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; // output channel index (each thread handles K channels?) // Actually, let me think about block dimensions } ``` Hmm, block dimensions and grid layout need careful thought. For 1x1 conv, the computation is extremely regular. Let me use: - Block size: (128, 1) or (32, 8) - need to balance - Each thread handles one spatial pixel and multiple output channels Wait, occupancy matters. On H100, each SM can have up to 2048 threads (or 32 thread blocks). With 128 threads per block, we can have 16 blocks per SM. Register usage matters too. Let me try a different layout. Process the output as [B, C_out, H, W]. We'll parallelize: - x-dimension: spatial locations (B×H×W) - y-dimension: output channels (C_out) A block of (32, 8) threads: - Each thread computes 1 output element - Total: 32 spatial locations × 8 output channels per block - For C_out=1280, we'd need 160 blocks in y - For B×H×W=10×120×120=144,000, we'd need 4,500 blocks in x That's 720,000 blocks total, which is plenty for GPU parallelism. But each thread only does C_in FMAs. For C_in=1152, that's ~1152 FMAs = ~2.3K FLOPs. Not a lot of work per thread, but with 720K threads... the issue is memory bandwidth. Actually, a better approach: have each thread compute K output channels for one spatial location. Then K=4 or K=8. Block of (32, 8) where: - threadIdx.x: spatial offset within block - threadIdx.y: output channel group within block - Each thread handles 8 output channels? No, threadIdx.y would be the output channel tile. Let me simplify: ``` // Launch: grid((B*H*W + 63)/64, (C_out + 7)/8) // Block: (64, 8) // Each thread computes 1 spatial location and 1 output channel int sp = blockIdx.x * 64 + threadIdx.x; // 0 to B*H*W-1 int oc = blockIdx.y * 8 + threadIdx.y; // 0 to C_out-1 ``` Each thread reuses the same input activations across threads in the same block, but they all need different weights. Actually, threads with different `oc` need different weight rows. Weight reads per block: 8 rows × C_in floats. If C_in is large, weights won't all fit in L1 cache for a block. But they're read sequentially by threadIdx.y dimension, so there's some locality. A better improvement: have threadIdx.y threads load weights cooperatively into shared memory! ``` // Block: (32, 8) // Shared memory: 8 * C_in floats (for 8 output channels) // But C_in can be 1152, so 8 * 1152 * 4 = 36 KB. That's fine! ``` Wait, 8 × 1152 = 9,216 floats = 36.9 KB. For smaller C_in, even less. That's very reasonable for shared memory. But what if a block handles more than 8 output channels? Like 64 output channels? That would be 64 × 1152 × 4 = 294 KB, too much. So: each block handles a tile of output channels (say 8 or 16) and a tile of spatial locations. For block (32, 8): - 32 spatial locations, 8 output channels - Shared memory for weights: 8 × C_in floats - Each of the 8 threads in y-dimension loads one row of C_in weights - Then all 32×8 threads compute using those weights Wait, but that means each spatial location computes 8 output channels. For 32 spatial locations, we need 32 threads in x to compute in parallel. The 8 threads in y load weights, then all 256 threads compute? No, simpler: - Block (32, 8) - threadIdx.y is the output channel within the tile of 8 - We load all 8×C_in weights into shared memory cooperatively - Each thread computes one (spatial_location, output_channel) pair - But all threads with the same threadIdx.y compute different spatial locations for the SAME output channel Yes, this works! The shared memory load pattern: - Thread (tx, ty) loads weight[ty][tx], weight[ty][tx+32], etc. in a strided pattern, or better, each thread loads a chunk. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. With C_in and 8 output channels, the weight matrix is [8][C_in]. We'd load this into shared memory as `float sw[8][C_in]`. A challenge: C_in might not be divisible by the number of threads. But C_in in this network is always divisible by 32 or at least 16. For the EfficientNet channels: 32, 16, 24, 40, 80, 112, 192, 320, 1280, with expansions: 32, 96, 144, 240, 480, 672, 1152. 32/32 = 1, 96/32 = 3, 144/32 = 4.5 (not divisible!) Hmm, 144 = 32×4 + 16. That's annoying. Actually, we could just have a loop where each thread loads floats one at a time without worrying about exact divisibility. Or we can pad. To keep things simple, let me make the shared memory loading generic with boundary checks, and make C_in a runtime parameter. But this is getting complex. Let me try an even simpler approach first: just have each thread compute one output element with a loop over C_in. For 1x1 conv, this is very straightforward. We can add shared memory optimization later if needed. Here's my proposed kernel signature for 1x1: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_bn_kernel( const float* input, // [B, C_in, H, W] const float* weight, // [C_out, C_in] const float* bn_w, // [C_out] const float* bn_b, // [C_out] const float* bn_mean, // [C_out] const float* bn_var, // [C_out] float* output, // [B, C_out, H, W] int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, float eps, int relu_type // 0=none, 1=relu, 2=relu6 ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * H * W; if (idx >= total) return; // Decode index int w = idx % W; int h = (idx / W) % H; int oc = (idx / (W * H)) % C_out; int b = idx / (W * H * C_out); // Compute dot product over C_in float sum = 0.0f; int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; int w_base = oc * C_in; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ic++) { sum += input[in_base + ic * H * W] * weight[w_base + ic]; } // BN float norm = (sum - bn_mean[oc]) / sqrtf(bn_var[oc] + eps); float out = norm * bn_w[oc] + bn_b[oc]; // Activation if (relu_type == 1) out = fmaxf(0.0f, out); else if (relu_type == 2) out = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, out), 6.0f); output[idx] = out; } ``` Wait, index calculation: if output is [B, C_out, H, W], then idx = ((b * C_out + oc) * H + h) * W + w. So: ``` w = idx % W tmp = idx / W h = tmp % H tmp = tmp / H oc = tmp % C_out b = tmp / C_out ``` And input offset for (b, ic, h, w) is ((b * C_in + ic) * H + h) * W + w. This kernel is very simple but might be memory bandwidth bound. Each thread reads C_in input floats + C_in weight floats + 5 BN floats + writes 1 output float. The weight reads for the same oc will be cached across threads in the same warp, but different warps computing different oc might evict each other. For C_in = 1152 and many output channels, the weights are 1280 × 1152 × 4 = ~5.7 MB. That easily fits in L2 cache! So weight reads for the second and subsequent warps should be L2 cache hits. Actually, wait. The total weight for a layer might be smaller than L2 cache. H100 has 50MB L2 cache. So most weight matrices will be cached after the first read. This means the memory traffic is primarily: - Read input activations: B×C_in×H×W floats - Read weights: first time only, then cached - Write output: B×C_out×H×W floats - Read BN params: negligible So the main bottleneck is reading input and writing output. This is the same as PyTorch's conv (which also reads input and writes output), plus PyTorch reads weights too. So our custom kernel should have very similar memory bandwidth to PyTorch's conv, but we save the bandwidth of the intermediate conv output + BN output. Actually, for PyTorch's separate conv + BN: - Conv: read input + read weights + write conv_output - BN: read conv_output + write bn_output Total reads: input + weights + conv_output Total writes: conv_output + bn_output For our fused kernel: - Read input + read weights + write output We save reading and writing conv_output! That's a 2× reduction in intermediate traffic. But wait, does our custom kernel really have comparable compute efficiency to cuDNN conv? If cuDNN is 2× faster at conv than our naive loop, then they could still win even with the extra memory traffic. But for 1x1 conv, cuDNN calls cuBLAS, which is extremely optimized. A naive loop might be 5-10× slower! However, on H100 with huge caches and bandwidth, and since 1x1 conv is heavily memory bandwidth bound when C_in is large... a naive implementation might not be that bad. Let me think. For 1x1 conv with large C_in, the computation is a dot product. cuBLAS uses very sophisticated tiling, vectorized loads, and Tensor Cores. A naive implementation with scalar loads will be much slower. Can we do vectorized loads? Yes, `float4` allows loading 4 floats at once. If C_in is a multiple of 4, we can unroll by 4. Let me improve the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_bn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_w, const float* __restrict__ bn_b, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, float eps, int relu_type ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * H * W; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int tmp = idx / W; int h = tmp % H; int tmp2 = tmp / H; int oc = tmp2 % C_out; int b = tmp2 / C_out; float sum = 0.0f; const float* wptr = weight + oc * C_in; int in_offset = ((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; // Process C_in in chunks of 4 using float4 int c_in4 = C_in / 4 * 4; for (int ic = 0; ic < c_in4; ic += 4) { float4 inp = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(input + in_offset + ic * H * W); float4 wgt = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(wptr + ic); sum += inp.x * wgt.x + inp.y * wgt.y + inp.z * wgt.z + inp.w * wgt.w; } for (int ic = c_in4; ic < C_in; ic++) { sum += input[in_offset + ic * H * W] * wptr[ic]; } float norm = (sum - bn_mean[oc]) * rsqrtf(bn_var[oc] + eps); float out = norm * bn_w[oc] + bn_b[oc]; if (relu_type == 1) out = fmaxf(0.0f, out); else if (relu_type == 2) out = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, out), 6.0f); output[idx] = out; } ``` This is better with float4 loads. But the memory access pattern for input is strided by H*W between channels, which means consecutive threads (consecutive idx) access different spatial locations for the SAME output channel. Wait, consecutive idx correspond to consecutive w, then h, then oc, then b. So threads in a warp will mostly access adjacent w for the same oc. But as they loop over ic, they access `input + in_offset + ic * H * W`. So for a given warp computing the same oc but different w, when ic increments by 1, the access is offset by H*W floats. Are these accesses coalesced? For a warp of 32 threads computing 32 consecutive w values at the same (b, oc, h), their addresses are: - thread 0: base + 0 + ic*H*W - thread 1: base + 1 + ic*H*W - thread 2: base + 2 + ic*H*W ... These are consecutive! So a float4 load by each thread would load 4 consecutive floats. For 32 threads, that's 128 consecutive floats - perfect coalescing. But wait, the input layout is [B, C_in, H, W]. So for fixed b, ic, h, the W dimension is contiguous. Yes! So threads accessing different w are accessing contiguous memory. Perfect. For weights: all threads in a warp compute the same oc (usually, or nearby oc). They all load the same weight[oc * C_in + ic] or nearby weights. This is also very cache-friendly. This kernel might actually be reasonably efficient! But the `float4` loads from input require `in_offset + ic * H * W` to be aligned. Since `ic * H * W` might not be a multiple of 4... actually, H*W for various layers: - 120*120 = 14400 (divisible by 4) - 60*60 = 3600 (divisible by 4) - 30*30 = 900 (divisible by 4) - 15*15 = 225 (NOT divisible by 4!) - 8*8 = 64 (divisible by 4) For 15×15 = 225, `ic * 225` is not necessarily divisible by 4. For ic=0, in_offset is aligned (since base of tensor is aligned). For ic=1, in_offset + 225 is misaligned. `float4` loads require 16-byte alignment. For the 15×15 case, we can't use float4 for the channel-strided access. We'd need to fall back to scalar loads. Or we could process channels in groups where the starting channel is a multiple of 4, but ic * 225 gives offsets 0, 225, 450, 675... only 0, 450, 900, 1350 are divisible by 2, and only 0, 900 are divisible by 4. So we can only use float4 at most every 4 channels. Not great. We can either: 1. Don't use float4 for input, just use scalar (simpler) 2. Transpose the input first so channels are contiguous for the dot product 3. Use a tile-based approach For simplicity, I'll use scalar loads initially. We can optimize later if needed. Wait, but for the 1x1 conv, there's actually a much better approach that doesn't require custom CUDA: use `torch.mm` with properly reshaped inputs. And we can still fuse BN and ReLU in a separate kernel? But that doesn't fuse with the conv. Alright, let me take a step back and think about what's REALLY going to give speedup on H100. On H100, for a batch size of 10, small conv layers will be heavily kernel-launch-bound and underutilize the GPU. But the larger layers (later in the network) should be compute or memory bandwidth bound. A very pragmatic approach: since we can't easily beat cuDNN on standard convs, what if we: 1. Keep the 1x1 convs in PyTorch (they call cuBLAS and are very fast) 2. Write custom kernels for depthwise convs + initial 3x3 conv 3. Write custom kernels to fuse BN + activation after PyTorch convs But we can't fuse BN with PyTorch convs if we keep them separate... UNLESS we rewrite the model to absorb BN parameters into the conv weights during initialization. For inference, `Conv + BN` can be fused into a single conv by adjusting weights and bias: - New weight = weight * (gamma / sqrt(var + eps)) - New bias = - mean * (gamma / sqrt(var + eps)) + beta Then `Conv(X) = new_conv(X) + new_bias` (if conv originally had no bias) followed by activation. Wait! The reference model uses `bias=False` for all conv layers! So we CAN fuse Conv+BN into a single conv with bias! For each conv layer, we can compute: ``` scale = bn_weight / sqrt(bn_running_var + eps) fused_weight = conv_weight * scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) fused_bias = -bn_running_mean * scale + bn_bias ``` Then instead of `conv(x) -> bn(x)`, we do `conv(x, fused_weight, fused_bias)`. And PyTorch's conv with bias should be just as fast as conv without bias (cuDNN handles it natively). Then we can follow with just ReLU/ReLU6 as an elementwise kernel! This is brilliant! We don't need custom conv kernels. We just need: 1. Pre-fuse Conv+BN during `__init__` or first `forward()` 2. Replace `conv + bn + relu` with `F.conv2d(..., fused_weight, fused_bias) + relu` 3. For the relu, we can use `torch.clamp` or a custom kernel... actually PyTorch's ReLU and ReLU6 are already fused with the previous op in many cases, or at least very fast. But wait, PyTorch in eager mode might not fuse ReLU with conv. The conv output is written, then ReLU reads it and writes again. We could write a custom elementwise kernel that does `x = conv(x); relu(x)` in-place. Actually, for memory bandwidth: a conv output tensor is written, then ReLU reads and writes it. That's 2× the memory traffic of doing it in-place. But ReLU is extremely cheap memory-bandwidth-bound. PyTorch's `F.relu_` does in-place ReLU, which might already fuse read+write into one pass! Wait, `F.relu_(x)` modifies x in-place. But if x is a newly allocated tensor from conv, does PyTorch's conv output allocation support in-place mutation? Yes, `F.relu_(x)` does read x, apply relu, write x back. But since x is in memory, this is one read + one write of the same memory region. For PyTorch, `F.relu(x)` creates a new tensor. `F.relu_(x)` modifies in place. So `x = F.conv2d(...); x = F.relu_(x)` does: - conv: read input + weights, write x - relu_: read x, write x Total: read input + weights + read x + write x Whereas a fused conv+relu would be: - read input + weights, compute, write relu'd output directly Total: read input + weights + write x But can we actually do fused conv+relu in PyTorch? Not easily in eager mode. However, if we compute `x = F.conv2d(...)` then `x = custom_relu_kernel(x)`, our custom kernel reads x and writes a NEW tensor with ReLU applied. That's still read+write. What if our custom kernel does in-place relu? Then: - conv writes x - custom_relu reads x, writes x in place Still a read+write for relu. To truly fuse, we need custom conv. But the BN fusion alone is a big win because it saves a whole tensor round-trip. Wait, there's another optimization. The MBConv blocks are: ``` conv1x1 + bn + relu6 + depthwise3x3 + bn + relu6 + conv1x1 + bn ``` If we fuse conv1x1+bn into conv1x1_bias, then we have: ``` conv1x1_bias + relu6 + depthwise3x3_bias + relu6 + conv1x1_bias ``` where depthwise3x3_bias is depthwise conv with fused BN bias. Now, `relu6` after conv is just elementwise. We could still use PyTorch's `F.hardsigmoid` or `F.relu6`. And PyTorch might not fuse. But the elementwise ops are memory bandwidth limited and fairly cheap. For depthwise conv, since it's not a standard GEMM, can we fuse depthwise conv + BN? Since depthwise uses `groups=hidden_dim`, the output channels don't mix. So for each output channel, we just add a bias. PyTorch's `Conv2d` with `bias=True` already does this efficiently! So fusing BN into depthwise conv bias is also straightforward. So the fused model would be: ```python # Instead of: out = conv1(x) out = bn1(out) out = F.relu6(out) # Use: out = F.conv2d(x, fused_w1, fused_b1) out = F.relu6(out) ``` This eliminates all BN layers entirely! We just precompute fused weights and biases. But does this actually speed things up? In eager mode, PyTorch still executes conv and relu6 as separate kernels. The main speedup comes from: 1. Eliminating BN computation (which is its own kernel) 2. Eliminating memory traffic for BN intermediate BN is elementwise, so it's memory bandwidth bound. Eliminating it saves one read+write of the activation tensor. For the whole network, that could add up to 10-20% savings. But we also need to replace with a custom CUDA kernel to satisfy the problem requirements. What if we: 1. Pre-fuse all Conv+BN weights 2. Write a custom CUDA kernel that does depthwise conv more efficiently than cuDNN for small batch sizes 3. Write a custom CUDA kernel for the final avgpool+linear 4. For 1x1 convs, just use PyTorch's F.conv2d with fused bias, which is very fast Actually, let me reconsider whether writing a custom depthwise conv kernel is worthwhile. PyTorch's depthwise conv on CUDA uses cuDNN's grouped convolution. For depthwise with groups = channels, cuDNN performance can vary. For small spatial sizes and many channels, it might be OK. For larger spatial sizes, it might be less optimal than a custom kernel. Let me think about another option: implement the entire MBConv block for the layers with stride=1 as a single kernel, including 1x1 conv, relu6, depthwise, relu6, and 1x1 conv. For stride=2, the spatial dimensions change in the middle, making it harder. But earlier I showed that fusing the entire block without redundant computation requires intermediate storage. And with shared memory limits, we might need to spill to global. But what if we process the block in a streaming fashion? For MBConv with stride=1 (same spatial size throughout): We could process small spatial tiles, say 8×8 pixels at a time. - For the first 1x1 conv: compute output for the 8×8 tile. This requires input for 8×8. - Apply relu6 - For depthwise 3x3: compute output for a 6×6 tile inside the 8×8 (since 3x3 conv reduces valid region by 1 on each side). Or if padding=1, we still need the 8×8 region but can compute 8×8 output. - Apply relu6 - For second 1x1 conv: compute output for 8×8 But we need all channels. For hidden_dim=480 and tile=8×8: - Expansion output: 480×8×8 = 30,720 floats = 123 KB - Plus other buffers... within shared memory! Wait, MBConv5 at 15×15: tile of 8×8 needs expansion for slightly larger region because of padding. But with padding=1 and stride=1, we need 10×10 for 8×8 output. Actually no: for 3x3 conv with padding=1, output size = input size. So if we compute 8×8 output, we need 8×8 input (with padding handled by boundary conditions). But the 3x3 kernel accesses (h-1,h,h+1) × (w-1,w,w+1), so edge threads need neighbors outside the tile. With padding=1 in PyTorch, outside-border pixels are treated as 0. To handle this in a tile-based kernel, we'd need to load a halo region. For 8×8 output tile, we'd need 10×10 input tile. That's 100 pixels per channel. For hidden_dim=1152 (MBConv7): 1152 × 100 = 115,200 floats = 460.8 KB. That's too big for shared memory. But maybe we can use smaller tiles, like 4×4 output which needs 6×6 input. For 1152 channels: 1152 × 36 = 41,472 floats = 165.9 KB. Still too big. Smaller tile: 2×2 output needs 4×4 input. For 1152: 1152 × 16 = 18,432 = 73.7 KB. That's OK, plus some other shared memory usage. But a 2×2 tile with 1152 channels means each block computes 2×2×1152 = 4,608 output pixels. Each output pixel of the first 1x1 conv requires C_in FMAs. This could be a lot of compute per block. Actually, let me compute: for MBConv7's first 1x1 conv (192→1152) at 8×8: - To compute 2×2 output pixels for all 1152 channels - Each pixel: 192 FMAs - 4 pixels × 1152 channels = 4,608 pixels - Total FMAs: 4,608 × 192 = 884,736 FMAs - That's a reasonable amount of work per block. At 67 TFLOPS, a block would take ~13 microseconds. But occupancy would depend on how many blocks we can run in parallel. Total blocks for this layer: (10×8×8) / 4 = 160 blocks. That's not enough to saturate an H100! H100 has ~132 SMs (actually H100 SXM has 132 SMs, PCIe has 114). We need thousands of blocks for good occupancy. So tiles of 2×2 are too large if they process all channels. We need blocks to be finer-grained in the channel dimension too. Alternative: one block computes a subset of channels for the entire spatial region. For 8×8 with 10 batches = 640 pixels. One block could compute 8 channels for all 640 pixels. That's 5,120 pixels, each with 192 FMAs = ~1M FMAs. Total blocks: 1152/8 = 144 blocks. Still not enough. Or compute 1 channel for all 640 pixels: 640 blocks. Better. But 640 pixels × 192 FMAs = 123K FMAs per block. Maybe too little work. For 4 channels: 640 × 4 = 2,560 pixels per block. Total blocks: 1152/4 = 288. Still not great. For 8 channels: 640 × 8 = 5,120 pixels per block. Total blocks: 144. For 16 channels: 10,240 pixels per block. Total blocks: 72. This is tricky. On H100, we want at least 10,000+ threads running concurrently. With 128 threads per block, that's ~100 blocks minimum per SM to hit max occupancy, or 1,000+ blocks for the whole GPU. But if each block handles many pixels, we get fewer blocks. Actually, occupancy isn't about total blocks, it's about concurrent blocks per SM. On H100, each SM can run up to 32 blocks. With 132 SMs, that's 4,224 blocks max concurrent. But each block needs enough warps to hide latency. If a block only has 128 threads (4 warps), and the computation is memory-bound with not much instruction-level parallelism, we might want 8+ warps per block. OK, I think I'm overcomplicating this. Let me think about what would actually work for the competition. The competition expects us to write a CUDA kernel that's faster than PyTorch eager mode. On H100 with batch=10, PyTorch eager mode will: 1. Underutilize the GPU for many operations 2. Spend significant time in kernel launches 3. Have memory traffic from intermediate tensors A pragmatic, winning strategy might be: 1. ABSORB all BN parameters into conv weights/biases 2. Remove all BN layers 3. For 1x1 convs: these are very fast in PyTorch. Keep them but call them directly with absorbed bias. 4. For depthwise convs: these MIGHT be slower in PyTorch. But actually, on modern PyTorch/CUDA, depthwise convs are reasonably optimized via cuDNN. 5. For initial conv and final operations: could use custom kernels 6. Write a SINGLE custom CUDA kernel that does the entire remaining computation via a sequence of operations Wait, #6 is what I really want. A single kernel that takes the input and produces the output, doing all layers internally. This eliminates ALL intermediate memory traffic and ALL kernel launch overhead. To do this, the kernel would need to implement convolutions as it goes. Since each layer's output becomes the next layer's input, we need to store intermediate results somewhere. For a single kernel approach at the layer granularity: - We could have each thread block process one image and one spatial tile - But then early layers have large spatial dim and small channels; later layers have small spatial and large channels. Actually, what if we process the network in "waves" where each wave is one layer, launched as a separate kernel, but our kernels are fused conv+act? This gives us ~20 kernel launches instead of ~112. That's a reduction in launch overhead. And if we absorb BN into conv weights, each layer is just conv + activation. The activation is elementwise and we CAN write a custom kernel that does conv then immediately applies activation without writing the unactivated output. So our custom kernels would be: 1. `conv1x1_relu` / `conv1x1` (with fused bias) 2. `depthwise3x3_relu` / `depthwise3x3` 3. `conv3x3_relu` (initial) 4. `avgpool_linear` (final) This is still 6 kernel types, 20 kernel calls total. Let me reconsider implementing custom 1x1 conv. For 1x1 conv, the operation is literally a matrix multiplication. And I can implement a decent kernel. Here's my kernel design for 1x1 conv that I think could be reasonably fast: ```cuda template <int C_IN, int C_OUT> __global__ void conv1x1_kernel(...) { // Block size: (WARP_SIZE, 4) = (32, 4) // Grid: ((B*H*W + 31)/32, (C_OUT + 3)/4) // Each warp handles 32 spatial pixels and 1 output channel // Each block handles 32 spatial pixels and 4 output channels int sp_base = blockIdx.x * 32; int oc_base = blockIdx.y * 4; int lane = threadIdx.x; // 0-31 int oc_offset = threadIdx.y; // 0-3 int oc = oc_base + oc_offset; if (oc >= C_OUT) return; int sp = sp_base + lane; if (sp >= B*H*W) return; // Decode spatial int w = sp % W; int h = (sp / W) % H; int b = sp / (H * W); float sum = 0.0f; int in_base = ((b * C_IN + 0) * H + h) * W + w; // Load weights for this output channel const float* wptr = weight + oc * C_IN; // Compute dot product #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < C_IN; ic++) { sum += input[in_base + ic * H * W] * wptr[ic]; } // Apply bias and activation sum = sum + bias[oc]; if (act == 1) sum = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); else if (act == 2) sum = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, sum), 6.0f); output[((b * C_OUT + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = sum; } ``` Hmm, but using compile-time constants for C_IN and C_OUT means we'd need to compile a separate kernel for every layer configuration. With CUDA C++ templates, this could be done, but `load_inline` makes it a bit cumbersome. We can use the C++ preprocessor or switch statements. Actually, we can just use runtime values. The compiler might not unroll loops, but that's OK. For the block configuration, 32 threads in x (warp size) is good because: - We access contiguous memory in W dimension - A warp of 32 threads reading 32 consecutive w values is perfectly coalesced And 4 output channels per block means the block has 128 threads, which is a nice size. With block=(32, 4, 1), and grid covering all spatial and output channels, each block computes 4 output channels for 32 spatial locations. Total blocks = ceil(B*H*W/32) * ceil(C_OUT/4). For B=10, H=W=15, C_OUT=112: spatial = 2250. Blocks_x = 71. Blocks_y = 28. Total blocks = 1,988. With 132 SMs, that's ~15 blocks per SM. Each block has 4 warps. Concurrent warps per SM on H100 is up to 64. So we get good occupancy. For the largest layer, B=10, H=W=8, C_OUT=320: spatial = 640. Blocks_x = 20. Blocks_y = 80. Total blocks = 1,600. Similar occupancy. For B=10, H=W=120, C_OUT=16: spatial = 144,000. Blocks_x = 4,500. Blocks_y = 4. Total blocks = 18,000. Lots of parallelism! This seems reasonable. Let's also think about the depthwise kernel. For depthwise 3x3: ```cuda __global__ void depthwise3x3_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int padding, int act ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; int tmp2 = tmp / H_out; int c = tmp2 % C; int b = tmp2 / C; float sum = 0.0f; const float* wptr = weight + c * 9; for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; dy++) { for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; dx++) { int in_h = h * stride + dy - padding; int in_w = w * stride + dx - padding; if (in_h >= 0 && in_h < H && in_w >= 0 && in_w < W) { sum += input[((b * C + c) * H + in_h) * W + in_w] * wptr[dy * 3 + dx]; } } } sum += bias[c]; if (act == 1) sum = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); else if (act == 2) sum = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, sum), 6.0f); output[idx] = sum; } ``` This is very straightforward. Each thread handles one output pixel for one channel. For C=1152, H_out=W_out=8, B=10: total threads = 10 * 1152 * 8 * 8 = 737,280. Each thread does 9 loads from input and 9 FMAs (or fewer for boundaries). Memory access pattern: consecutive threads (consecutive idx) access consecutive w values for the same c, b, h. When they load input, they load at offsets that differ by `stride` in W. For stride=1, consecutive threads load contiguous memory (with some warp divergence at boundaries, which is minor for large tensors). For the initial 3x3 conv (32→32, actually wait: initial is 3→32, k=3, s=2, p=1): ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_kernel(...) { // Similar to depthwise but without groups // Each output channel depends on all input channels // C_in = 3, C_out = 32 for initial layer } ``` Since C_in=3, each thread can compute one output pixel across all 32 output channels. We read the 3×3×3 input window once (27 floats) and compute 32 outputs. For the adaptive_avg_pool + linear, we can do: ```cuda __global__ void avgpool_linear_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int num_classes ) { int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; // Compute avgpool: average over H*W for each channel // Then compute linear: output[b][oc] = sum_c pooled[c] * weight[oc][c] + bias[oc] } ``` Actually, for adaptive_avg_pool2d with output=1x1, it's just averaging all spatial pixels per channel. So we get C values per batch item. Then dot with weight matrix. We can have each block handle one batch item. Within the block, threads can cooperatively compute the averages: ```cuda __shared__ float pooled[1280]; // max channels // Each thread computes partial sum for some channels and some spatial pixels // Then reduce within block // Then compute linear output ``` For 1280 channels and 8×8=64 spatial pixels, total elements = 81,920 per batch. With 256 threads, each thread handles ~320 elements. Hmm, or we can use a reduction tree. Actually, adaptive_avg_pool is trivial: for each channel, sum all H*W pixels and divide by H*W. With 1280 channels and 64 pixels, that's 81,920 operations per image. Then linear: 1000 × 1280 = 1.28M FMAs per image. For batch=10, total linear FMAs = 12.8M. At 67 TFLOPS, that's 0.19 ms. The overhead of two kernel launches is probably comparable. Fusing them makes sense mainly to reduce launches. For the fused kernel, we can compute the avgpool to shared memory, then compute the linear output. Since there are only 1000 output classes, and each is a dot product of 1280 values, we could have each thread compute one or more output classes. Actually, for the classifier, a very simple kernel: ```cuda __global__ void classifier_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int num_classes ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int oc = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y * blockDim.x; if (oc >= num_classes) return; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float avg = 0.0f; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { for (int w = 0; w < W; w++) { avg += input[((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w]; } } avg /= (H * W); sum += avg * weight[oc * C + c]; } sum += bias[oc]; output[b * num_classes + oc] = sum; } ``` But this computes the average for each channel 1000 times (once per output class)! That's very redundant. Better to precompute averages. Improved: ``` Block handles one batch item b. Thread idx computes: if (threadIdx.x < C) { // Compute average for channel threadIdx.x float avg = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { // Actually, blockDim.x might be < C } } __syncthreads(); // Then compute linear using the averages ``` Let me just precompute averages in shared memory, then do the GEMV: ```cuda __global__ void avgpool_linear_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int num_classes ) { __shared__ float pooled[1280]; // assuming max C = 1280 int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Each thread computes avgpool for some channels for (int c = tid; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; int base = ((b * C) + c) * H * W; for (int i = 0; i < H * W; i++) { sum += input[base + i]; } pooled[c] = sum / (H * W); } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes some output classes for (int oc = tid; oc < num_classes; oc += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[oc]; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum += pooled[c] * weight[oc * C + c]; } output[b * num_classes + oc] = sum; } } ``` With blockDim.x = 256 and C = 1280: - First loop: each thread computes avg for ~5 channels. - Second loop: each thread computes ~4 output classes. Each output class needs 1280 FMAs. For batch=10, we'd launch 10 blocks, each with 256 threads. That's very few blocks for an H100. But each block does ~5K FMAs for avgpool + ~5K FMAs for linear = ~10K FMAs total. At 67 TFLOPS, 10K FLOPs is 0.15 microseconds per block. Kernel launch overhead might be comparable. For larger efficiency, we could have each block handle multiple batch items. Say blockDim.x = 256, and each block handles 4 batch items, with 64 threads per item. But 64 threads for 1280 channels means 20 channels per thread for avgpool, and 16 output classes per thread for linear. That's similar total work. Or we could just use PyTorch's `F.adaptive_avg_pool2d` and `self.fc`. For 10 images, this is negligible compared to the conv layers. OK, I realize the classifier is a tiny fraction of the FLOPs. The real work is in the conv layers. Let me now estimate the FLOPs for each layer to see where time is spent: Initial conv: 3→32, k=3, s=2, H=W=240→120 FLOPs = 10 * 32 * 120 * 120 * 3 * 3 * 3 = 124.4M MBConv1: 32→16, expand=1, s=1 - 1x1: 32→32 at 120×120 = 10 * 32 * 120 * 120 * 32 = 147.5M - dw 3×3 s=1: 32 at 120×120 = 10 * 32 * 120 * 120 * 9 = 41.5M - 1x1: 32→16 at 120×120 = 10 * 16 * 120 * 120 * 32 = 73.7M Total: 262.7M MBConv2: 16→24, expand=6→96, s=2 - 1x1: 16→96 at 120×120 = 10 * 96 * 120 * 120 * 16 = 221.2M - dw 3×3 s=2: 96 at 60×60 = 10 * 96 * 60 * 60 * 9 = 31.1M - 1x1: 96→24 at 60×60 = 10 * 24 * 60 * 60 * 96 = 82.9M Total: 335.2M MBConv3: 24→40, expand=6→144, s=2 - 1x1: 24→144 at 60×60 = 10 * 144 * 60 * 60 * 24 = 124.4M - dw 3×3 s=2: 144 at 30×30 = 10 * 144 * 30 * 30 * 9 = 11.7M - 1x1: 144→40 at 30×30 = 10 * 40 * 30 * 30 * 144 = 51.8M Total: 187.9M MBConv4: 40→80, expand=6→240, s=2 - 1x1: 40→240 at 30×30 = 10 * 240 * 30 * 30 * 40 = 86.4M - dw 3×3 s=2: 240 at 15×15 = 10 * 240 * 15 * 15 * 9 = 4.9M - 1x1: 240→80 at 15×15 = 10 * 80 * 15 * 15 * 240 = 43.2M Total: 134.5M MBConv5: 80→112, expand=6→480, s=1 - 1x1: 80→480 at 15×15 = 10 * 480 * 15 * 15 * 80 = 86.4M - dw 3×3 s=1: 480 at 15×15 = 10 * 480 * 15 * 15 * 9 = 9.7M - 1x1: 480→112 at 15×15 = 10 * 112 * 15 * 15 * 480 = 120.9M Total: 217.0M MBConv6: 112→192, expand=6→672, s=2 - 1x1: 112→672 at 15×15 = 10 * 672 * 15 * 15 * 112 = 169.3M - dw 3×3 s=2: 672 at 8×8 = 10 * 672 * 8 * 8 * 9 = 3.9M - 1x1: 672→192 at 8×8 = 10 * 192 * 8 * 8 * 672 = 82.8M Total: 256.0M MBConv7: 192→320, expand=6→1152, s=1 - 1x1: 192→1152 at 8×8 = 10 * 1152 * 8 * 8 * 192 = 141.6M - dw 3×3 s=1: 1152 at 8×8 = 10 * 1152 * 8 * 8 * 9 = 6.6M - 1x1: 1152→320 at 8×8 = 10 * 320 * 8 * 8 * 1152 = 235.9M Total: 384.1M Final conv: 320→1280, k=1 at 8×8 = 10 * 1280 * 8 * 8 * 320 = 262.1M FC: 1280→1000 = 10 * 1000 * 1280 = 12.8M Total FLOPs: ~1.94B FLOPs The largest layers by FLOPs are: 1. MBConv7 projection: 235.9M 2. Final conv: 262.1M 3. MBConv1 expansion+projection: 147.5 + 73.7 = 221.2M 4. MBConv6 expansion: 169.3M 5. Initial conv: 124.4M Interestingly, the depthwise convs are tiny in FLOPs! The 1x1 convs dominate, accounting for about 75% of the FLOPs. This means if we want speedup, we MUST have fast 1x1 conv implementations, because that's where most compute is. For 1x1 conv, PyTorch calls cuBLAS, which is highly optimized. Unless we use Tensor Cores or very good shared memory tiling, we won't beat cuBLAS. But wait! Maybe we don't need to beat cuBLAS on each individual 1x1 conv. What if we FUSE multiple 1x1 convs together? For example, in an MBConv block with stride=1: ``` input → 1x1_expand → relu6 → 1x1_project ``` Actually, the depthwise is in between and it's not a 1x1. But what if we do: ``` 1x1_expand(x) → store in buffer depthwise3x3(buffer) → relu6 → store in buffer 1x1_project(buffer) ``` This is what PyTorch does too. What if we fuse the two 1x1 convs with the depthwise into one giant kernel? We showed earlier that computing the whole block per output pixel causes redundant computation. But what about a tile-based approach with global memory for intermediates? That doesn't save memory bandwidth over separate kernels. Unless... what if we process the block at the tile level, where the tile is small enough that the expansion output fits in shared memory? MBConv5 at 15×15 with 480 channels: we need 480 channels × tile_size. If tile_size = 15×15 = 225, that's 108,000 floats = 432 KB. Too big for shared memory (227 KB). But what if tile_size covers just a few pixels? For 4×4 = 16 pixels, 480 channels = 7,680 floats = 30.7 KB. That fits! And for depthwise with padding=1, we need a 6×6 region of the expansion, which is 480 × 36 = 17,280 floats = 69.1 KB. Still fits! But wait: if we compute 4×4 output pixels for the projection, we need the depthwise output for 4×4 pixels, which requires 4×4 = 16 pixels of the expansion output after depthwise (since depthwise is s=1, p=1). Actually, depthwise with padding=1 outputs the same spatial size. So if we compute 4×4 output pixels for the final projection conv, we need 4×4 pixels from the depthwise output. The depthwise output was computed from a 6×6 region of the expansion output. So we need to compute the expansion output for 6×6 pixels. For a block computing a 4×4 tile of final output: 1. Compute 1x1 expansion for 6×6 region = 36 pixels × 480 channels = 17,280 floats. But we're computing 480 channels × 36 pixels. Can we store this in shared memory? 69 KB yes! 2. Apply ReLU6 to the 69 KB in shared memory 3. Compute depthwise for 4×4 output from 6×6 input. Each of 480 channels: 4×4×9 = 144 FMAs per channel. Total: 480×144 = 69,120 FMAs. 4. Apply ReLU6 to depthwise output. 4×4×480 = 7,680 floats = 30.7 KB. 5. Compute 1x1 projection from 4×4×480 to 4×4×112. Each output pixel: 112 × 480 = 53,760 FMAs. 16 pixels: 860,160 FMAs. Total FMAs per block for this tile: - Expansion: 36 × 480 × 80 = 1,382,400 (for C_in=80) - Depthwise: 69,120 - Projection: 860,160 Total: ~2.3M FMAs That's quite a bit of compute per block! And we save writing the expansion and depthwise outputs to global memory. For 15×15 spatial with 10 batch: total output pixels = 10 × 112 × 15 × 15 = 252,000. With 4×4 tiles: 252,000 / 16 = 15,750 blocks. That's a lot of blocks! Each with 128 threads: 15,750 × 128 = 2,016,000 threads. Good parallelism. But shared memory is 69 KB for expansion + 31 KB for depthwise output = 100 KB. That's OK. Wait, actually: the expansion is 6×6 = 36 pixels per channel, 480 channels. But we process this for a specific batch index. So for each block (which handles 1 batch, some spatial tile, all channels): - Shared memory: 480 × 36 × 4 = ~69 KB (expansion) - Shared memory: 480 × 16 × 4 = ~30.7 KB (depthwise output) - actually we can write depthwise output directly to registers or a smaller shared buffer - Actually, we might not need to store depthwise output in shared memory at all! We can compute the projection on the fly. For projection 1x1: each output channel is a weighted sum across all 480 input channels for that pixel. So for pixel (h, w), the projection output for channel oc is: ``` proj_out[oc] = sum_c depthwise_out[c][h][w] * weight[oc][c] ``` We can compute this by accumulating in registers for all 112 output channels at once. But 112 registers per thread is a lot! Each thread in the block might handle one or a few output channels for all pixels in the tile. Let me rethink the block structure: - A block handles ONE spatial tile (e.g., 4×4) for ONE batch item - Within the block, threads are organized as (channels, outputs) Actually, a cleaner structure: - Block size: (16, 8) where x=16 for spatial pixels in tile, y=8 for output channels - Block handles a 4×4 spatial tile (16 pixels) and 8 output channels of the projection - For each of the 8 output channels: - We need to compute the dot product over 480 depthwise channels for each of 16 pixels - The dot product for one pixel and one output channel is 480 FMAs - One thread handles 1 pixel and 1 output channel: 480 FMAs - Total threads per block: 16 × 8 = 128 threads - Total work per block: 16 pixels × 8 channels × 480 = 61,440 FMAs just for projection For the depthwise part: - The 16 threads in x-dimension (one per pixel) all need the same 480 depthwise channels but for different pixels - We can compute depthwise on the fly from the expansion buffer in shared memory - Or precompute depthwise for the 16 pixels and store in another shared buffer For the expansion part: - We need to compute expansion for all 480 channels for the 6×6 region (if depthwise requires it) - The 16 threads then load from this shared memory for the depthwise Wait, but if we only compute depthwise for 16 output pixels (4×4 tile), and depthwise is 3×3 with padding=1, we actually need the expansion for 4×4 + padding = up to 6×6. But with careful boundary handling, yes. But actually, different output pixels in a 4×4 tile need overlapping 3×3 regions of the expansion. The union is a 6×6 region (or slightly less at image boundaries). So we compute expansion for 6×6 and store in shared memory. To compute expansion for 6×6 with 480 channels and C_in=80: - 36 pixels × 480 channels × 80 FMAs = 1,382,400 FMAs - With 128 threads: ~10,800 FMAs per thread - Plus depthwise: 16 pixels × 480 channels × 9 FMAs = 69,120 FMAs (~540 per thread) - Plus projection: 16 × 8 × 480 = 61,440 FMAs (~480 per thread) Total: ~1.5M FMAs per block. At 67 TFLOPS, if we achieve 10% efficiency, that's ~0.2 ms per block. With 15,750 blocks for the whole layer = ~3 seconds. That's way too slow! But many blocks run in parallel. Actually, 15,750 blocks with 128 threads each = 2M threads. At a time, H100 can run ~132 SMs × ~2,048 threads/SM = 270K threads. So it would take ~8 "waves" of blocks. At 0.2 ms per block... hmm, 0.2 ms per block is actually huge. But that's at 10% of 67 TFLOPS. For 1.5M FMAs, at 10% efficiency that's 6.7 GFLOPS, so 1.5M / 6.7B = 0.22 ms. 8 waves × 0.22 ms = 1.76 ms for just the MBConv5 block. Meanwhile, PyTorch's cuBLAS does the two 1x1 convs at maybe 0.1 ms each? And depthwise at 0.05 ms? Total ~0.25 ms. So our fused kernel is 7× slower. This means our custom kernel must be much more efficient. The issue is that 128 threads doing 1.5M FMAs is a lot of work but poor memory coalescing and instruction-level parallelism. For cuBLAS on 1x1 conv 80→480 at 15×15: it reshapes to GEMM with M=B×H×W=2250, N=480, K=80. cuBLAS achieves near-peak TFLOPS on this. The 1.4M FMAs take maybe 0.05 ms. So unless our entire fused block achieves cuBLAS-level performance on the 1x1 conv parts, it's hopeless. This is a crucial insight: **for 1x1 convs, we cannot beat cuBLAS with a simple custom kernel, and fusing them with other ops inside a block doesn't help if the 1x1 conv is the bottleneck.** So the only winning strategy for 1x1 convs is... to use PyTorch's `F.conv2d` or `torch.matmul`! And focus our custom CUDA on things where we CAN win. Where can we win? 1. **Elementwise ops**: fusing ReLU6 with something else 2. **Depthwise convs**: maybe we can write a better kernel than cuDNN for some cases 3. **Final classifier**: fusing avgpool + linear could help 4. **Kernel launch overhead**: use fewer kernels overall But if 1x1 convs account for 75%+ of compute time and we use PyTorch for those, can we still win? Let's think. In eager mode, PyTorch does: ``` out = conv1x1(x) # kernel 1 out = bn(out) # kernel 2 out = relu6(out) # kernel 3 (if not fused with bn) out = depthwise(out) # kernel 4 out = bn(out) # kernel 5 out = relu6(out) # kernel 6 out = conv1x1(out) # kernel 7 out = bn(out) # kernel 8 ``` If we absorb BN into conv weights and use in-place ReLU6, this becomes: ``` out = conv1x1(x, fused_bias) # kernel 1 out = relu6_(out) # kernel 2 out = depthwise(out, fused_bias) # kernel 3 out = relu6_(out) # kernel 4 out = conv1x1(out, fused_bias) # kernel 5 ``` That's 5 kernels per MBConv block instead of 8. Here's a standard approach: ```cuda // Tile sizes #define TM 8 #define TN 8 #define TK 16 __global__ void gemm_1x1( const float* A, // [M, K] where M = B*H*W const float* B, // [K, N] where N = C_out float* C, int M, int N, int K ) { __shared__ float As[TM][TK]; __shared__ float Bs[TK][TN]; int bx = blockIdx.x, by = blockIdx.y; int tx = threadIdx.x, ty = threadIdx.y; // Each block computes a TM×TN tile of C // But actually, we should have multiple threads per tile // Simpler: each thread computes one output element // Use shared memory for a tile of A and B } ``` This is a standard GEMM kernel.
