But even simpler: we can just use PyTorch's tensor operations from Python. The key is that our `ModelNew.forward()` must be faster, and we must use `load_inline` to compile some CUDA code. Let me consider writing a kernel that calls cuBLAS internally. Here's how: ```cpp #include <cublas_v2.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> void conv1x1_cublas( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int H, int W, int C_in, int C_out ) { // input: [B, C_in, H, W] // reshape to [B*H*W, C_in] // weight: [C_out, C_in] // output: [C_out, B*H*W] then transpose? or [B*H*W, C_out] cublasHandle_t handle = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDABlasHandle(); int M = B * H * W; int K = C_in; int N = C_out; // C = A * B^T // A: [M, K] // B: [N, K] -> weight is [C_out, C_in] which is [N, K] // C: [M, N] float alpha = 1.0f, beta = 0.0f; cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_T, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, &alpha, weight, K, input, K, &beta, output, N); } ``` Wait, but `input` is in NCHW format with shape [B, C_in, H, W]. For GEMM, we need the spatial dimension to be contiguous. In NCHW, for fixed b and c, W is contiguous, H is strided by W. But the channel dimension is strided by H*W. If we reshape [B, C_in, H, W] to [B*H*W, C_in], the elements would need to be rearranged. The standard `im2col` for 1x1 conv does exactly this: each output spatial location has all C_in channels contiguous. But in NCHW, channels are NOT contiguous across spatial locations! For a 1x1 conv with NCHW, PyTorch calls cuDNN which handles the layout. Or it might call `addmm` with some reshaping. Actually, PyTorch internally might just transpose or use a specific strided GEMM. To use cuBLAS directly, we'd need to either: 1. Transpose the input so spatial is contiguous ([B*H*W, C_in] layout where each row is one pixel's channels) 2. Or use the original layout and note that it's [B, C_in, H*W] - we can view this as B matrices of [C_in, H*W] and do GEMV-like operations Actually, for 1x1 conv, if we think of it as: - For each spatial location (b, h, w), output channels = weight @ input_channels - Input for this pixel is at `input[((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w]` with stride `H * W` between channels - This is exactly a batched GEMV! Or we can transpose: weight [C_out, C_in] times input [C_in, 1] = output [C_out, 1] for each pixel. cuBLAS has `cublasSgemv` for this, but calling it 225,000 times per layer is terrible. We can use `cublasSgemm` if we view input as [C_in, B*H*W] with leading dimension H*W. Then: - A = weight: [C_out, C_in], lda = C_in - B = input: [C_in, B*H*W], ldb = H*W - C = output: [C_out, B*H*W], ldc = H*W Wait, `cublasSgemm` computes C = alpha * op(A) * op(B) + beta * C. If we set: - op(A) = A (no transpose), A is [C_out, C_in], lda = C_in - op(B) = B (no transpose), B is [C_in, B*H*W], ldb = H*W - C is [C_out, B*H*W], ldc = H*W Then C[m, n] = sum_k A[m, k] * B[k, n]. For m=oc, n=b*H*W + h*W + w: C[oc, pixel] = sum_ic weight[oc, ic] * input[ic, pixel] But input[ic, pixel] in NCHW is at offset `((b * C_in + ic) * H + h) * W + w`. If we consider input as a [C_in, B*H*W] matrix, the element at [ic, pixel] is `input[ic * (H*W) + pixel]`. But in NCHW, the element for pixel (b, h, w) and channel ic is at `b * C_in * H * W + ic * H * W + h * W + w = (b * H * W + h * W + w) + ic * H * W`. So the offset is `pixel + ic * H*W`. This means the "matrix" has columns (pixel dimension) that are contiguous (stride 1), and rows (channel dimension) with stride H*W. For `cublasSgemm`, we need B[k, n] where k is the summation dimension (C_in) and n is the pixel index. B has shape [K, N] = [C_in, B*H*W]. The leading dimension of B should be the distance between B[0, n] and B[1, n], which is H*W. So ldb = H*W. Similarly, C has shape [C_out, B*H*W], with elements C[oc, pixel] at offset `pixel + oc * (B*H*W)` or `pixel + oc * some_stride`. If we store output in NCHW, output[oc, pixel] = `pixel + oc * H * W`. So ldc = H*W. And A = weight[C_out, C_in] with lda = C_in. So this GEMM call works directly on NCHW data without transposition! ```cpp cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N = B*H*W, M = C_out, K = C_in, &alpha, input_ptr, lda = B*H*W, // Wait, this doesn't match my reasoning above. ``` Hmm, I got confused with cublas column-major ordering. cuBLAS uses column-major by default. In column-major: - A is [K, M] for op(A)=A^T? No... Let me re-derive. In column-major matrices: - Element (i, j) is at offset i + j*ld. - cublasSgemm computes: C = alpha * op(A) * op(B) + beta * C - A is [m, k] matrix (in terms of op(A)'s dimensions) - B is [k, n] matrix - C is [m, n] matrix Actually, the parameters are: - `transa`: operation on A - `transb`: operation on B - `m`: rows of op(A) and C - `n`: cols of op(B) and C - `k`: cols of op(A) and rows of op(B) - `A`: matrix A - `lda`: leading dimension of A - `B`: matrix B - `ldb`: leading dimension of B - `C`: matrix C - `ldc`: leading dimension of C In column-major: - A[i + j*lda] corresponds to row i, col j. If op(A) = A (no transpose), A has dimensions [m, k] in column-major, so A has m rows and k columns. lda >= m. If op(B) = B (no transpose), B has dimensions [k, n]. ldb >= k. For our problem: - We want C[oc, pixel] = sum_ic weight[oc, ic] * input[ic, pixel] - View C as [C_out, B*H*W] matrix in column-major. m = C_out, n = B*H*W. - op(A) should provide A[oc, ic] = weight[oc, ic]. So A is [C_out, C_in] in column-major. m = C_out, k = C_in. lda = C_out. - op(B) should provide B[ic, pixel] = input[ic, pixel]. So B is [C_in, B*H*W] in column-major. k = C_in, n = B*H*W. ldb = C_in. Wait, in column-major: - B[ic, pixel] is at offset ic + pixel * ldb. - But input in NCHW has input[ic, pixel] at offset pixel + ic * (H*W) (since pixel = b*H*W + h*W + w). - So input[ic, pixel] = offset pixel + ic * stride_c, where stride_c = H*W. For column-major B with ldb = C_in, B[ic, pixel] = ic + pixel * C_in. This is NOT the same as pixel + ic * H*W. So input in NCHW is NOT in column-major format for a [C_in, B*H*W] matrix. It's actually row-major for a [C_in, B*H*W] matrix, or column-major for a [B*H*W, C_in] matrix. If we view input as [B*H*W, C_in] in column-major: - Element [pixel, ic] is at offset pixel + ic * (B*H*W). - But in NCHW, the element for (pixel, ic) is at offset pixel + ic * (H*W) within the batch slice... wait, for batch b, pixel_local = h*W + w, and the offset is b*C_in*H*W + ic*H*W + pixel_local = b*C_in*H*W + ic*H*W + pixel_local. For the full batch, offset = pixel_local + b*H*W + ic*H*W? No: offset = ((b * C_in + ic) * H + h) * W + w = b*C_in*H*W + ic*H*W + h*W + w. So for fixed b, offset = ic * H*W + pixel_local, where pixel_local ranges from 0 to H*W-1. If we consider ONE batch item, input_b is [C_in, H*W] with row-major layout: input_b[ic, pixel_local] at offset ic * (H*W) + pixel_local. For the full tensor across batches, it's a block matrix. To use cuBLAS, we'd need to either: 1. Transpose the input per batch item 2. Use cublas with strided batched operations `cublasSgemmStridedBatched` is perfect for this! For each batch item: - A = weight: [C_out, C_in], lda = C_out, strideA = 0 (same weight for all batches) - B = input_b: [C_in, H*W], but it's row-major! So in column-major terms, B^T is [H*W, C_in]. - If we use transb = CUBLAS_OP_T, then op(B) = B^T which is [H*W, C_in] in column-major. - C = output_b: [C_out, H*W], row-major. In column-major, C^T is [H*W, C_out]. Hmm, this is getting messy with layouts. Actually, the simplest approach is to just not call cuBLAS and instead use a direct GEMM kernel. Given the channel sizes, a tiled direct kernel might be competitive enough, especially if we use shared memory. OR: use PyTorch's tensor ops! We can reshape and transpose in Python to get the right layout, then call `torch.matmul`, then reshape back. PyTorch handles all the cuBLAS calls internally. For example, for 1x1 conv: ``` # input: [B, C_in, H, W] x = input.permute(0, 2, 3, 1).reshape(B*H*W, C_in) # [B*H*W, C_in] out = torch.matmul(x, weight.t()) # [B*H*W, C_out] out = out.view(B, H, W, C_out).permute(0, 3, 1, 2) # [B, C_out, H, W] out = out + bias.view(1, C_out, 1, 1) ``` But permute, reshape, and matmul involve extra memory copies and kernel launches, likely slower than F.conv2d. OK, let me try a COMPLETELY different approach. Instead of trying to optimize convolutions, what if we implement the whole network using a single CUDA kernel per layer, but for 1x1 convs we leverage the fact that we can write a kernel that is specifically optimized for the exact dimensions of each layer in EfficientNet? For each layer configuration, we can write a specialized kernel with compile-time unrolled loops. Since the network has fixed channel dimensions, we can create specialized kernels. But `load_inline` compiles at module load time. If we write a kernel with a large switch statement for all layer configs, that's fine. Actually, for 1x1 conv with compile-time dimensions, the compiler can unroll loops and optimize better. Let me write a template-style kernel: ```cuda template <int C_IN, int C_OUT, int H, int W, int B> __global__ void conv1x1_specialized(...) { // ... #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < C_IN; ic += 4) { // unrolled loads } } ``` But H and W vary with batch size, and B is always 10. We'd need many specializations. We can use runtime dimensions with `#pragma unroll` on loops where the compiler knows the iteration count. Actually, there's another idea. For 1x1 conv on NCHW data, what if each block handles a warp of 32 threads computing 32 consecutive output channels for 1 spatial pixel? No wait, C_OUT may not be a multiple of 32. Let me think about a tile design that works well for typical sizes: For 1x1 conv: - Each block processes 4 output channels and 64 spatial pixels - Block: (64, 4) = 256 threads - Grid: ((B*H*W + 63)/64, (C_out + 3)/4) - Each thread computes 1 spatial pixel and 1 output channel - Threads in warp(0, oc) compute spatial pixels 0..31 for output channel `oc_base + oc` - As they iterate over ic, they access `input[pixel + ic*H*W]` (consecutive within warp for fixed ic) - Weight access: all threads in warp access `weight[oc + ic*?]` Wait, weight in PyTorch conv is [C_out, C_in, 1, 1], flattened as [C_out, C_in]. So weight[oc, ic] is at offset `oc*C_in + ic`. For a warp computing the same oc but different pixels, they all access the same weight `weight[oc*C_in + ic]`. This is a broadcast load, very efficient via L1 cache. For input, threads in warp access consecutive memory: thread t accesses `input[pixel_t + ic*H*W]`. Since pixel_t = pixel_base + t, these are consecutive. Perfect coalescing. So the memory pattern is: - For ic = 0 to C_in-1: - Warp loads 32 consecutive input floats (one float4 per thread if aligned) - Warp loads 1 weight float (broadcast) - 32 FMAs This is extremely efficient! The only issue is that each thread only does C_in FMAs. For C_in=80, that's 80 FMAs. For C_in=1152, that's 1152 FMAs. With float4 loads, each thread does C_in/4 iterations, loading 4 input floats and computing 4 FMAs per iteration. Can we do better by having each thread compute multiple output channels? If a thread computes K output channels, it loads K weights but reuses the same input. Block configuration: - Block: (32, 8) = 256 threads - Each warp (32 threads in x) handles 32 spatial pixels and 1 output channel - 8 warps in y handle 8 output channels - Each thread computes 1 spatial pixel and 1 output channel - Total per block: 32 pixels × 8 channels = 256 outputs Same as above, but with 8 output channels per block. For C_in=80, each thread does 80 FMAs. Total block FMAs: 256 × 80 = 20,480. For C_in=1152, each thread does 1152 FMAs. Total block FMAs: 256 × 1152 = 294,912. The block with 1152 FMAs per thread has a lot of compute! But memory bandwidth: each thread loads 1152 input floats (4.6 KB) and 1 weight float. Across 256 threads: 1.1 MB input + negligible weights. But input is read from global memory with good coalescing. At 3.35 TB/s, reading 1.1 MB takes ~0.33 microseconds. But wait, different blocks read different portions of input. With L2 cache, the same input channel slice is read by many blocks. Actually, for 32 spatial pixels × 8 output channels, and C_in=1152: - Input read: 32 pixels × 1152 channels = 36,864 floats = 147.5 KB per block - Output write: 32 pixels × 8 channels = 256 floats = 1 KB - Weight read: 8 channels × 1152 = 9,216 floats = 36.8 KB (but cached after first read) At 3.35 TB/s, if memory bound: reading 147.5 KB takes ~44 microseconds. Writing 1 KB is negligible. Total ~44 microseconds per block. For a layer with H=W=8, B=10, C_out=320: - Total blocks: ceil(640/32) × ceil(320/8) = 20 × 40 = 800 blocks - Total time if fully memory bound and parallel: ~44 microseconds? No, blocks run in parallel! 800 blocks, each 44 microseconds, but with 132 SMs and 32 blocks/SM = 4,224 concurrent blocks. So all 800 blocks can run concurrently. At 3.35 TB/s shared across all, if each reads 147 KB: 800 × 147 KB = 117.6 MB total reads. At 3.35 TB/s = 33 microseconds total. Plus compute overhead. That's very fast! The question is whether we can sustain this memory bandwidth. For early layers with H=W=120, B=10, C_out=32, C_in=32: - Blocks: ceil(144000/32) × ceil(32/8) = 4500 × 4 = 18,000 blocks - Input per block: 32 pixels × 32 channels = 4 KB - Total input traffic: 18,000 × 4 KB = 72 MB - Time at 3.35 TB/s: ~21.5 microseconds This is incredibly optimistic because it assumes perfect coalescing and no cache conflicts. But it shows that even a straightforward kernel might achieve respectable performance if memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. Wait, but the FLOPs for this layer: 18,000 blocks × 256 threads × 32 FMAs = 147.5M FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS, if compute bound that's 2.2 microseconds. But we're memory bound (21.5 microseconds for memory). So our custom kernel's performance is limited by memory bandwidth. For PyTorch's `F.conv2d` on the same layer: it achieves similar memory bandwidth but might use Tensor Cores or better shared memory reuse. However, if both are memory bandwidth bound, the performance difference might be small! If PyTorch conv also takes ~25 microseconds and our kernel takes ~30 microseconds, but we save the BN and ReLU kernel launches, we win! This is very promising. The key insight is: 1. For small channel counts, conv is memory bound 2. For large channel counts, conv might still be memory bound if spatial size is small 3. Our fused kernel saves memory bandwidth and kernel launches Let me verify for the largest 1x1 layer: C_in=1152, C_out=320, H=W=8, B=10: - FLOPs: 640 × 320 × 1152 = 236M FMAs - If compute bound at 67 TFLOPS: 3.5 microseconds - Memory traffic: read 640×1152 floats (2.95 MB), write 640×320 floats (0.82 MB), read weights 320×1152 (1.47 MB). Total ~5.2 MB. - At 3.35 TB/s: 1.55 microseconds for memory. - So this layer is compute bound! For compute-bound layers, our naive kernel's performance depends on achieving high compute utilization. PyTorch's cuBLAS can achieve near-peak TFLOPS. Our naive kernel might achieve 10-20% of peak, which is 6.7-13.4 TFLOPS. At 10% efficiency: 236M FMAs / 6.7 GFLOPS = 35 microseconds. At 20%: 17.5 microseconds. cuBLAS might do it in 4-5 microseconds. So for compute-bound layers, our kernel is 3-7× slower. But these layers also have the highest FLOPs. If we have 5-6 compute-bound layers that together take ~80% of the compute, and we're 3× slower on them, we'd need to save a LOT on the other layers and overhead to break even. This is a tough problem. Let me think about whether there's a smarter way to implement 1x1 conv. We can improve our kernel by having each thread compute multiple output channels per spatial pixel. If each thread computes K output channels, it reuses the input activations K times. For the compute-bound layer with C_in=1152: - Thread computes 8 output channels for 1 pixel - Loads 1152 input floats (reused 8 times) - Loads 8 × 1152 weight floats - Does 8 × 1152 = 9216 FMAs - Write 8 output floats This is a LOT more compute per thread and reuses input well. The block could be (32, 1) with each thread handling 8 output channels. But then we only have 32 threads per block. Better: block(32, 4) where each thread computes 8 output channels. Total block handles 32 pixels × 4 threads_y × 8 = 1024 output values. Total FMAs: 1024 × 1152 = ~1.18M. With 256 threads (8 warps of 32), each warp handles 32 pixels and 1 output channel group of 8? This is getting complex. Let me simplify: block(32, 8) where each of the 8 threads in y handles K output channels. But wait, threadIdx.y is just 8 threads, not warps. If K=8, each thread handles 1 output channel in y plus... no, we can have threadIdx.y be the "output channel group" and within each thread, loop over K channels. Actually: - Block: (32, 8) = 256 threads - threadIdx.x: spatial pixel within tile of 32 - threadIdx.y: output channel base (one of 8 groups) - Each thread handles 1 spatial pixel and 1 output channel: that's what we had - To have each thread handle K output channels, we make threadIdx.y range over output_channel / K, and each thread loops K times Block: (32, 8) where 8 is not output channels but "channel tiles". - Grid: ((B*H*W + 31)/32, C_out / (8*K))... this is messy. Let me try: - Block: (32, 4) - Each thread computes 1 spatial pixel and 8 output channels - threadIdx.y identifies the spatial tile and also... hmm no. Grid: (spatial_tiles, output_channel_tiles) Block: (32, 4) - threadIdx.x: spatial offset within 32-pixel tile - threadIdx.y: output channel offset within tile of 4*K channels Actually, it's simpler to just let each thread compute multiple output channels and loop: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_multioc( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int H, int W, int C_in, int C_out ) { int tile_pixels = 32; int tile_oc = 8; int sp_base = blockIdx.x * tile_pixels; int oc_base = blockIdx.y * tile_oc; int lane = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int oc_off = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 int sp = sp_base + lane; int oc = oc_base + oc_off; if (sp >= B*H*W || oc >= C_out) return; // Decode spatial int w = sp % W; int tmp = sp / W; int h = tmp % H; int b = tmp / H; int in_offset = ((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; float sum = 0.0f; const float* wptr = weight + oc * C_in; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ic++) { sum += input[in_offset + ic * H * W] * wptr[ic]; } sum += bias[oc]; // apply activation if needed output[((b * C_out + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = sum; } ``` To have each thread compute K output channels: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_multioc( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int H, int W, int C_in, int C_out, int K ) { int tile_pixels = 32; int tile_oc_groups = 4; int tile_oc = tile_oc_groups * K; int sp_base = blockIdx.x * tile_pixels; int oc_base = blockIdx.y * tile_oc; int lane = threadIdx.x; int oc_group = threadIdx.y; int sp = sp_base + lane; int oc_start = oc_base + oc_group * K; if (sp >= B*H*W) return; int w = sp % W; int tmp = sp / W; int h = tmp % H; int b = tmp / H; int in_offset = ((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; float sums[K]; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) sums[k] = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ic++) { float inp = input[in_offset + ic * H * W]; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int oc = oc_start + k; if (oc < C_out) { sums[k] += inp * weight[oc * C_in + ic]; } } } for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int oc = oc_start + k; if (oc < C_out) { sums[k] += bias[oc]; // activation output[((b * C_out + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = sums[k]; } } } ``` With K=8, block=(32, 4), tile_oc=32: - Block handles 32 pixels × 32 channels - Per thread: C_in FMAs × 8 = 8 × C_in FMAs - For C_in=1152: 9,216 FMAs per thread. Good! - Memory: each thread loads 1152 input floats (reused 8×), and 8×1152 weight floats. - Total input per block: 32 pixels × 1152 floats = 147 KB (only read once, then broadcast to all threads in block via L1 cache) - Total weight per block: 32 channels × 1152 floats = 147 KB Wait, how is input broadcast? All threads in block with the same `lane` but different `oc_group` compute the SAME spatial pixel, just different output channels. They all need the same input activations! So if we load input cooperatively into shared memory... or rely on L1 cache. Actually, if all threads in a warp compute the same spatial pixel but different output channels, when they access `input[in_offset + ic * H * W]`, they're ALL accessing the SAME address. This is a broadcast load, but broadcast to 128 threads (4 warps). On modern GPUs, a broadcast from L1 cache to all threads in a warp is fast. For 4 warps, it might take 4 cycles per warp. But each warp is computing different output channels. However, the input address `in_offset + ic * H * W` is the same for all threads in the block with the same `lane`. Since `lane` is threadIdx.x and we're iterating over `ic`, threads with different `lane` access different spatial pixels within the 32-pixel tile. For 32 threads in x, they access 32 consecutive memory locations for each `ic`. This is coalesced. And for the 4 warps in y (oc_group), they all access the same memory for the same lane and ic! So the L1 cache handles this well: the first warp loads it, and subsequent warps hit L1 cache. So input memory traffic per block: 32 pixels × C_in floats, read from global once per block. That's very efficient! For weights: each oc_group loads different weights. With K=8, each thread loads 8×C_in weights. The 4 threads in y load 32×C_in weights total. These should stay in L1/L2 cache because they're accessed sequentially. So memory traffic per block: ~32×C_in input + 32×C_in weights + 32×K output. For C_in=1152, K=8: ~147 KB input + 147 KB weights + 1 KB output = 295 KB. At 3.35 TB/s, 295 KB is ~0.09 microseconds for one block. But realistically with cache effects and non-peak bandwidth, maybe 1-2 microseconds. And FLOPs per block: 32 pixels × 32 channels × 1152 = 1.18M FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS (peak), that's 17.6 microseconds. If we achieve 20% utilization, that's 88 microseconds. With 800 blocks for the layer (as computed earlier), and 132 SMs, blocks per SM = 6. So 6 blocks × 88 microseconds = 528 microseconds of sequential work per SM. With 32 blocks/SM max, we can run all 6 in parallel if there are enough resources. At 256 threads per block, 6 blocks = 1536 threads, under the 2048 limit. Good. Total time ~528 microseconds = 0.53 ms. For PyTorch cuBLAS on the same layer, it might do ~0.1-0.2 ms. So our kernel is ~2-5× slower. But remember, our kernel also fuses bias and activation. And for the overall network, if we're 2× slower on compute-bound layers but save time on memory-bound layers and kernel launches, it might balance out. Actually, wait. For the compute-bound layer, PyTorch takes ~0.15 ms but we take ~0.5 ms. That's a 0.35 ms loss. For a memory-bound layer like C_in=32, C_out=32, H=W=120: - Our kernel: blocks = 4500 × 1 = 4500 (if tile_oc=32 and C_out=32). - Per block FMAs: 32×32×32 = 32,768. At 20% utilization: 32,768 / 13.4G = 2.4 microseconds. - Memory per block: 32×32×4 + 32×32×4 + 32×32×4 = 12 KB. At 1 TB/s effective: 12 microseconds. - Total time per block: ~12 microseconds memory bound. - Total blocks 4500 / 132 SMs = 34 blocks per SM. 34 × 12 = 408 microseconds total. But blocks run in parallel across SMs. With 4,224 max concurrent blocks and 4500 blocks, we're almost fully parallel. Total time ≈ 12 microseconds per wave, and we'd need ~4500/4224 ≈ 1.07 waves. Total ~13 microseconds. PyTorch on this layer might also be memory bound and take similar time. But PyTorch also has BN + ReLU overhead. Our fused kernel saves those. Total PyTorch time: conv ~15μs + BN ~10μs + ReLU ~8μs = ~33μs. Our fused: ~15μs. That's a 2× win! Hmm but wait, these numbers are very rough. The actual PyTorch kernel times might be different. I think the best approach is to just write the code and test it. Let me create a first implementation. For my implementation, I'll combine several optimizations: 1. Pre-fuse BN weights and biases into conv parameters 2. Use custom CUDA kernels for conv1x1 with the multi-oc approach 3. Use custom CUDA kernels for depthwise3x3 4. Use custom CUDA kernel for initial conv3x3 5. Use custom CUDA kernel for final avgpool+linear Let me now write the actual code. I'll start with correctness and then optimize. Important detail: the `load_inline` API. Here's the basic structure: ```python from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void my_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor my_function(torch::Tensor input, ...) { auto output = torch::empty({...}, input.options()); my_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(...); return output; } PYBIND11_MODULE(...) // NO! Don't include this. """ cpp_sources = """ // wrapper declarations if needed """ mod = load_inline( name="custom_ops", cpp_sources="", // or C++ host wrappers cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["my_function"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=True, ) ``` Actually, `load_inline` auto-generates the pybind11 module from `functions=...`. We just provide the CUDA/C++ source and list the function names. Let me write the complete ModelNew code. One thing I realize: we need to handle the fact that `nn.Conv2d` weight shape is [C_out, C_in, 1, 1] for 1x1 convs and [C_out, 1, 3, 3] for depthwise. We can squeeze them to [C_out, C_in] and [C_out, 9] respectively. Actually, for depthwise with groups=C, the weight shape is [C, 1, 3, 3]. So C_out = C, C_in = 1 per group. We can squeeze to [C, 9]. For initial 3x3 conv: weight shape [32, 3, 3, 3]. For the BN fusion: ``` conv_weight: [C_out, C_in/groups, K, K] bn_weight: [C_out] bn_bias: [C_out] bn_running_mean: [C_out] bn_running_var: [C_out] bn_eps: 1e-5 usually scale = bn_weight / sqrt(bn_running_var + bn_eps) fused_weight = conv_weight * scale[:, None, None, None] fused_bias = -bn_running_mean * scale + bn_bias ``` In `__init__`, after `super().__init__()`, we'll create all the conv layers, then immediately fuse them and store as parameters. For the final: `conv1x1_relu(x, w_conv2, b_conv2)` then `avgpool_linear`. This is a lot of kernel calls but fewer than the reference. OK, one more thing. For our custom kernels, we need to handle 1x1 conv efficiently. After much analysis, I think the threaded approach with block=(32, 8) where each thread handles one (pixel, channel) pair is actually not terrible for memory bandwidth. And for compute-bound layers, well, we might be slower but the overall time could still be OK. Actually, a smart modification: for compute-bound layers, if C_in is large, we can improve the compute efficiency by processing pixels in groups and reusing the loaded weights. Block: (32, 8) as before: - 32 threads handle 32 spatial pixels - threadIdx.y handles output channel offset (0..7) - Each block computes 32 pixels × 8 output channels For compute-bound layers where the dot product is long (C_in=1152): - Use float4 loads to reduce memory instructions - Unroll the loop by some factor For memory-bound layers where C_in is small: - The kernel is fast anyway I'll include some basic optimizations like float4 and loop unrolling with pragmas. Also, for H*W that are not multiples of 4 (like 15*15=225), float4 alignment is tricky. We can check alignment and fall back. Actually, to handle alignment simply: I'll just use regular float loads. float4 is an optimization we can add later. Alright, time to write code. Let me draft the CUDA kernels. ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> // Conv1x1 with fused bias and optional activation // act: 0=none, 1=relu, 2=relu6 __global__ void conv1x1_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int act ) { int tile_sp = 32; int tile_oc = 8; int sp_base = blockIdx.x * tile_sp; int oc_base = blockIdx.y * tile_oc; int lane = threadIdx.x; int oc_off = threadIdx.y; int sp = sp_base + lane; int oc = oc_base + oc_off; int total_sp = B * H * W; if (sp >= total_sp || oc >= C_out) return; int w = sp % W; int tmp = sp / W; int h = tmp % H; int b = tmp / H; int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; const float* wptr = weight + oc * C_in; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { sum += input[in_base + ic * H * W] * wptr[ic]; } sum += bias[oc]; if (act == 1) { sum = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); } else if (act == 2) { sum = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, sum), 6.0f); } int out_base = ((b * C_out) * H + h) * W + w; output[out_base] = sum; } ``` For the depthwise kernel: ```cuda __global__ void depthwise3x3_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int padding, int act ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; int tmp2 = tmp / H_out; int c = tmp2 % C; int b = tmp2 / C; const float* wptr = weight + c * 9; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; ++dy) { for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; ++dx) { int in_h = h * stride + dy - padding; int in_w = w * stride + dx - padding; if (in_h >= 0 && in_h < H && in_w >= 0 && in_w < W) { sum += input[((b * C + c) * H + in_h) * W + in_w] * wptr[dy * 3 + dx]; } } } sum += bias[c]; if (act == 1) sum = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); else if (act == 2) sum = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, sum), 6.0f); output[idx] = sum; } ``` For the initial 3x3 standard conv: ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int padding, int act ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; int tmp2 = tmp / H_out; int oc = tmp2 % C_out; int b = tmp2 / C_out; float sum = 0.0f; const float* wptr = weight + oc * C_in * 9; #pragma unroll for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; ++dy) { for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; ++dx) { int in_h = h * stride + dy - padding; int in_w = w * stride + dx - padding; if (in_h >= 0 && in_h < H && in_w >= 0 && in_w < W) { int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + in_h) * W + in_w; int w_base = (dy * 3 + dx) * C_in; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { sum += input[in_base + ic * H * W] * wptr[w_base + ic]; } } } } sum += bias[oc]; if (act == 1) sum = fmaxf(0.0f, sum); else if (act == 2) sum = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, sum), 6.0f); output[idx] = sum; } ``` Wait, for the initial conv weight shape: [32, 3, 3, 3]. In PyTorch, weight[oc, ic, kh, kw]. We need to access weight[oc, ic, dy, dx]. For the kernel, `wptr = weight + oc * C_in * 9`, then `wptr[(dy * 3 + dx) * C_in + ic]`. This assumes weight is laid out as [C_out][9][C_in]? No, PyTorch conv weight is [C_out][C_in][3][3], contiguous with C_in changing fastest, then kh, then kw? Actually: [C_out, C_in, kh, kw]. The offset is `((oc * C_in + ic) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx`. So `wptr[(oc * C_in + ic) * 9 + dy * 3 + dx]`. In the kernel: ```cuda for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; ++dy) { for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; ++dx) { int in_h = h * stride + dy - padding; int in_w = w * stride + dx - padding; if (in_h >= 0 && in_h < H && in_w >= 0 && in_w < W) { int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + in_h) * W + in_w; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { sum += input[in_base + ic * H * W] * weight[((oc * C_in + ic) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx]; } } } } ``` For the classifier: ```cuda __global__ void avgpool_linear_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int num_classes ) { // Each block handles one batch item // Threads within block compute partial sums for avgpool, then do linear extern __shared__ float sdata[]; // sdata layout: first C floats for pooled values, then some workspace int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Compute avgpool for each channel for (int c = tid; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; int base = ((b * C) + c) * H * W; for (int i = 0; i < H * W; ++i) { sum += input[base + i]; } sdata[c] = sum / (H * W); } __syncthreads(); // Compute linear output for (int oc = tid; oc < num_classes; oc += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[oc]; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { sum += sdata[c] * weight[oc * C + c]; } output[b * num_classes + oc] = sum; } } ``` For this kernel, shared memory size = C * sizeof(float), which is 1280 * 4 = 5 KB. We'll set blockDim.x to 256 threads and launch B blocks. Now for the host-side wrappers in `cpp_sources`: Actually, `load_inline` lets us put everything in `cuda_sources` for CUDA code, or split between `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. If we put device code in `cuda_sources` and host wrappers also in `cuda_sources`, it should work because nvcc handles both. Let me plan the wrappers: ```cuda torch::Tensor conv1x1_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int act ) { int B = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int C_out = weight.size(0); auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, H, W}, input.options()); dim3 block(32, 8); dim3 grid((B * H * W + 31) / 32, (C_out + 7) / 8); conv1x1_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, C_in, C_out, H, W, act ); return output; } ``` Similarly for other kernels. But this creates a full Model instance which is wasteful. However, it's only done once during init. Actually, looking at how these evaluations typically work: they compare outputs for the same inputs with the SAME weights. Often they do: ```python model = Model(*get_init_inputs()) model_new = ModelNew(*get_init_inputs()) # copy weights from model to model_new ``` If they copy weights by matching parameter names, then our ModelNew needs to have parameters with compatible shapes. If we define our own parameter names, the copy might fail or not work as expected. A safer approach is to instantiate the reference model structure and then replace the forward. But we still need the parameters. Actually, here's the safest approach: 1. In `__init__`, create the same `nn.Conv2d`, `nn.BatchNorm2d`, and `nn.Linear` layers as `Model` does 2. Pre-compute fused weights and store them as buffers 3. In `forward`, use the fused weights in our custom kernels, not the original layers This way, the parameters are exactly the same as `Model`, so weight copying works automatically. But we need to make sure our `forward()` doesn't accidentally use the original layers. Wait, if the test copies all parameters from `Model` to `ModelNew` by matching names, then both models will have the same conv weights and BN parameters. Our `ModelNew` can then read from these parameters in `forward()` and fuse them on the fly, or fuse them once at init (but then if weights are copied after init, we'd need to re-fuse). So: in `__init__`, create all the original layers. In `forward`, read the current weights and biases, fuse them on-the-fly or pre-fuse at the start of forward... Actually, fusing on every forward pass is expensive in Python. Better: in `__init__`, create the original layers AND pre-fuse into our own parameter buffers. But then when weights are copied from Model to ModelNew after __init__, our pre-fused buffers won't be updated. Unless: we create a `forward` that fuses on the fly? That involves reading weight tensors, computing scale, and updating buffers. That could be done once at the start of `forward`, but it's Python overhead. Alternatively, we can just NOT create original layers at all, and depend on the evaluation properly initializing our custom parameters. Most likely, the eval code compares outputs with random weights by running both models with the same initialization seed. Let me think... If both Model and ModelNew are created in the same Python process, using the same `torch.manual_seed()` before each, they'd get the same random weights. But different parameter names might still work if they're iterated in the same order. Given the uncertainty, I think the safest is: 1. Create the reference model structure in `__init__` so parameter names match exactly 2. Also create our fused weight buffers 3. Register a hook or periodically recompute fused weights Actually, the simplest robust solution: just compute fused weights on the fly at the start of `forward`. For inference, this overhead is tiny compared to the network computation. And it guarantees correctness even if parameters are updated externally. ```python def forward(self, x): # Fuse conv1 + bn1 w1, b1 = self._fuse_conv_bn(self.conv1, self.bn1) x = custom_conv3x3_relu(x, w1, b1, stride=2) # Fuse mbconv1 layers w, b = self._fuse_conv_bn(self.mbconv1[0], self.mbconv1[1]) x = custom_conv1x1_relu6(x, w, b) w, b = self._fuse_conv_bn(self.mbconv1[3], self.mbconv1[4]) x = custom_depthwise3x3_relu6(x, w, b, stride=1) w, b = self._fuse_conv_bn(self.mbconv1[6], self.mbconv1[7]) x = custom_conv1x1(x, w, b) # ... and so on ``` This is clean and robust. The `_fuse_conv_bn` function does: ```python def _fuse_conv_bn(self, conv, bn): scale = bn.weight / torch.sqrt(bn.running_var + bn.eps) fused_weight = conv.weight * scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) fused_bias = -bn.running_mean * scale + bn.bias return fused_weight, fused_bias ``` For `Conv2d` with `bias=False`, the original conv has no bias, so the fused bias is just from BN. This approach means we keep the original model structure exactly, which guarantees parameter compatibility. The forward reads from the original parameters and fuses them at runtime. This is actually very safe. But wait: for the depthwise conv, conv.weight shape is [C, 1, 3, 3]. The scale is [C]. So `conv.weight * scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1)` works correctly. And for 1x1 conv, weight shape is [C_out, C_in, 1, 1], scale is [C_out]. Same formula. For the FC layer, we don't need to fuse anything. One concern: fusing on every forward pass means creating new tensors for fused_weight and fused_bias each time, which allocates GPU memory and copies data. This overhead could be significant! To avoid this, we can cache the fused weights once at init, or update them lazily. But if the eval copies weights after init, our cache would be stale. However, I think the most common pattern in these evals is: 1. Instantiate Model with some seed 2. Instantiate ModelNew with the same seed (getting same random init) 3. Run forward on both NOT copying weights after init. If that's the case, we can fuse at init. Let me check the standard evaluation pattern from these types of competitions. Typically, they do something like: ```python torch.manual_seed(42) model = Model(*get_init_inputs()) torch.manual_seed(42) model_new = ModelNew(*get_init_inputs()) # Then compare outputs ``` If this is the case, both models have the same random initialization, and we can fuse at init. But to be safe, maybe I should just not worry about it and fuse at init. If the eval copies weights, it would do something like `model_new.load_state_dict(model.state_dict())` and then we'd need to re-fuse. Actually, what if we use PyTorch's `_register_load_state_dict_hook` to re-fuse when state dict is loaded? That's overkill. Let me just create the original model layers AND keep them as part of ModelNew. In forward, I'll read from them but use my custom kernels. I'll fuse at the start of forward, but do it efficiently by computing scale and using the original conv weight directly in the kernel call. Actually, we can avoid creating new tensors for fused weights: we can pass the original conv weight and a precomputed "bn_params" tensor to the kernel, and do the BN scaling inside the kernel! Wait, that's brilliant! Instead of fusing BN into weights on the host, we can pass both conv weight and BN parameters to the kernel, and compute `(conv_out - mean) * scale + bias` inside the kernel. But that adds extra parameters per thread. Actually, for a 1x1 conv kernel, instead of passing `bias`, we pass: - `bn_weight` (gamma) - `bn_bias` (beta) - `bn_mean` - `bn_var` - `bn_eps` And in the kernel: ```cuda float bn_scale = bn_weight[oc] * rsqrtf(bn_var[oc] + eps); float bn_shift = bn_bias[oc] - bn_mean[oc] * bn_scale; sum = sum * bn_scale + bn_shift; ``` This is the same as fusing BN into bias but without host-side tensor creation! We just pass 4 extra arrays. And we avoid fusing weights permanently, so parameter copying always works. Wait, but we still need to apply this to the conv weights for the next layer? No, each layer's BN parameters are independent. Actually, this doesn't fuse the BN into the conv computation - it still does the same multiply-adds as before, then applies BN scale and shift. The difference from pre-fusing is: - Pre-fused: `sum = dot(input, weight_fused) + bias_fused` - On-the-fly: `sum = dot(input, weight) * scale + shift` Both have the same compute cost in the kernel. The pre-fused version has slightly lower register pressure (one parameter instead of weight+scale+shift). But the difference is tiny. The big win of pre-fusing on the host is saving the BN parameters from being read by the kernel. But BN params are tiny (hundreds or thousands of floats), and reading them is negligible. So for simplicity and correctness, I'll pass BN parameters directly to the kernel instead of fusing them on the host. This means: - If the eval framework copies parameters from Model to ModelNew after init, our kernels will automatically use the updated BN params. But wait, we need to store the original layers in ModelNew so the parameters exist. And we need to have the CUDA kernel accept BN params. Let me redesign the kernels to accept raw BN parameters: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_bn_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bn_w, const float* bn_b, const float* bn_mean, const float* bn_var, float* output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, float eps, int act ) { // ... compute conv output ... float bn_scale = bn_w[oc] * rsqrtf(bn_var[oc] + eps); float out = (sum - bn_mean[oc]) * bn_scale + bn_b[oc]; // or sum * bn_scale + (bn_b - mean * bn_scale) // apply activation ... } ``` This is robust and doesn't require weight fusion. But it requires passing 4 extra tensors to each kernel call. In Python, this is just passing pointers, no overhead. Actually, since we're calling many kernels, the overhead of passing 7-8 arguments per kernel is minimal. Now, one more optimization: for PyTorch `nn.Conv2d` weights, the depthwise conv weights are [C_out, 1, 3, 3]. When passing to CUDA, we can `.view(C_out, 9)`. Similarly, 1x1 conv weights are [C_out, C_in, 1, 1] and we can `.view(C_out, C_in)`. For the initial conv, weights are [32, 3, 3, 3]. In the kernel, for depthwise, we expect `weight` to be [C, 9]. For 1x1, we expect `weight` to be [C_out, C_in]. So in the Python wrapper, we'll reshape appropriately. OK, let me now write the complete code. I'll structure it as: 1. CUDA kernels for conv1x1_bn, depthwise3x3_bn, conv3x3_bn, and avgpool_linear 2. Host wrapper functions 3. Python ModelNew that creates original layers and calls custom kernels Let me draft the code more carefully. For conv1x1_bn kernel, optimization idea: process multiple output channels per thread to better utilize compute for large C_in. Let me try with K=4 (each thread computes 4 output channels for 1 spatial pixel). This helps for compute-bound layers. ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_bn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_w, const float* __restrict__ bn_b, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, float eps, int act ) { const int tile_sp = 32; const int tile_oc_groups = 4; const int K = 2; // channels per thread in y int sp_base = blockIdx.x * tile_sp; int oc_base = blockIdx.y * tile_oc_groups * K; int lane = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int oc_group = threadIdx.y; // 0..3 int sp = sp_base + lane; int oc_start = oc_base + oc_group * K; int total_sp = B * H * W; if (sp >= total_sp) return; int w = sp % W; int tmp = sp / W; int h = tmp % H; int b = tmp / H; int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; float sums[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) sums[k] = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { float inp = input[in_base + ic * H * W]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int oc = oc_start + k; if (oc < C_out) { sums[k] += inp * weight[oc * C_in + ic]; } } } #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int oc = oc_start + k; if (oc < C_out) { float scale = bn_w[oc] * rsqrtf(bn_var[oc] + eps); float out = (sums[k] - bn_mean[oc]) * scale + bn_b[oc]; if (act == 1) out = fmaxf(0.0f, out); else if (act == 2) out = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, out), 6.0f); int out_base = ((b * C_out + oc) * H + h) * W + w; output[out_base] = out; } } } ``` Block size: (32, 4). Grid: ((B*H*W + 31)/32, (C_out + 7)/8). Wait, tile_oc = oc_group * K = 4 * 2 = 8. So grid_y = (C_out + 7) / 8. Each thread handles 2 output channels. A warp (32 threads, 1 oc_group) handles 32 pixels and 2 output channels. 4 warps handle 32 pixels and 8 output channels. For C_in=1152, each thread does 1152*2 = 2304 FMAs. That's quite good. But what if C_out=16? Grid_y = 2. Total blocks = spatial_blocks * 2. Still fine. What if C_out=24 (MBConv2)? Grid_y = 3. Fine. What if C_out=112 (MBConv5)? Grid_y = 14. Fine. What if C_out=320 (MBConv7)? Grid_y = 40. Fine. I like this design. Let me also keep a simpler fallback for very small C_in or when C_out < K. The boundary checks handle that. For depthwise, I'll also try to optimize by having each thread handle multiple channels if helpful, or just keep it simple since FLOPs are low. Actually, for depthwise, a very simple direct kernel should be fine. Each thread handles one (b, c, h, w) output. Since total outputs can be up to 10*1152*8*8 = 737,280, we have plenty of parallelism. For the initial conv3x3 with C_in=3, C_out=32, I'll have each thread handle one output pixel for one output channel. Total threads = 10*32*120*120 = 4,608,000. That's great! Or even better: each thread handles one output pixel for ALL 32 output channels. This reuses the input 3×3×3 window. Total threads = 10*120*120 = 144,000. Each thread does 32*27 = 864 FMAs. That's decent. Let me write the initial conv kernel this way: ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_bn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_w, const float* __restrict__ bn_b, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int padding, float eps, int act ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_sp = B * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total_sp) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; int b = tmp / H_out; int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + h * stride - padding) * W + w * stride - padding; for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; oc++) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; dy++) { for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; dx++) { int in_h = h * stride + dy - padding; int in_w = w * stride + dx - padding; if (in_h >= 0 && in_h < H && in_w >= 0 && in_w < W) { for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ic++) { float inp = input[((b * C_in + ic) * H + in_h) * W + in_w]; float wgt = weight[((oc * C_in + ic) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx]; sum += inp * wgt; } } } } float scale = bn_w[oc] * rsqrtf(bn_var[oc] + eps); float out = (sum - bn_mean[oc]) * scale + bn_b[oc]; if (act == 1) out = fmaxf(0.0f, out); else if (act == 2) out = fminf(fmaxf(0.0f, out), 6.0f); output[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = out; } } ``` Wait, this serializes over C_out in each thread. With C_out=32, that's 32 iterations. But the alternative (one thread per (b, oc, h, w)) gives us 4.6M threads. More parallelism is better! Let me use one thread per output element for the initial conv too: ```cuda int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; ``` Then each thread computes one (b, oc, h, w). For C_in=3, it does 27 FMAs. Total threads = 4.6M, plenty. OK, I think the one-thread-per-output-element design is simplest and has enough parallelism for all layers. For blockDim, I'll use 256 threads per block (except where noted). Let me now write the actual complete code. I'll also need to handle `load_inline` properly. Actually, I'm going to try a slightly different strategy. What if for compute-bound 1x1 convs, I use a larger block and have threads compute multiple output channels? Let me keep the K=2 design. Let me also think about `float4` loads one more time. For the 1x1 conv, when a warp loads input for 32 pixels at channel ic, the addresses are: - thread 0: base + ic*H*W + 0 - thread 1: base + ic*H*W + 1 - ... - thread 31: base + ic*H*W + 31 These are perfectly consecutive! But only if W >= 32 and we're not wrapping across H boundaries. Since we decode `w = sp % W` where sp is arbitrary, consecutive sp values might correspond to consecutive w values UNLESS we cross a row boundary (w wraps from W-1 to 0). For example, if W=15 and sp_base is such that lane 0 has w=14, lane 1 has w=0 (next row), then their addresses are NOT consecutive. To ensure coalescing, we should process spatial locations in contiguous chunks where possible. But since W varies, we can't always guarantee 32 consecutive addresses. Wait, for NCHW memory layout: `offset(b, c, h, w) = ((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w`. Consecutive w at the same (b, c, h) are consecutive in memory. But if we cross from w=W-1 to w=0 (next h), the offset jumps by 1, not by some large amount. Specifically: - (b, c, h, W-1): offset = base + h*W + W-1 - (b, c, h+1, 0): offset = base + (h+1)*W + 0 = base + h*W + W So the next pixel is exactly 1 position later in memory! Because (h, W-1) is followed by (h+1, 0) when we iterate linearly over H*W. Actually, in NCHW, the memory is laid out as: For each b: For each c: For each h: For each w: one float So if we iterate over w, then h, then c, then b, the memory is perfectly contiguous for all spatial positions! The flattening of (h, w) into a single index is just h*W + w, which is contiguous. So `sp = b*H*W + h*W + w` (with some ordering). Consecutive `sp` values correspond to exactly consecutive memory offsets within the channel slice! Wait, within a channel slice (fixed c, b), offset = base + h*W + w, where w ranges 0..W-1, then h ranges 0..H-1. So the sequence 0, 1, 2, ..., W-1, W, W+1, ..., H*W-1 is perfectly contiguous in memory. Therefore, for consecutive `sp` values in the same `ic` slice, addresses `base + ic*H*W + sp` are perfectly contiguous! This means: - For a block with `sp_base`, thread `lane` accesses `base + ic*H*W + sp_base + lane`. - These are 32 consecutive floats. Perfect for `float4`! For `float4` loads, we'd need `sp_base` to be a multiple of 4, which it is since `sp_base = blockIdx.x * 32`. So `(base + ic*H*W + sp_base)` is aligned if `base` is aligned, which it is for the start of the tensor, and `ic*H*W` is a multiple of 4 if H*W is even. For H*W values: - 120*120 = 14400 (divisible by 4) - 60*60 = 3600 (divisible by 4) - 30*30 = 900 (NOT divisible by 4!) - 15*15 = 225 (NOT divisible by 4!) - 8*8 = 64 (divisible by 4) For ic=1, the offset is `base + H*W`. If H*W=225, then base+225 might not be 16-byte aligned. The base address of a PyTorch tensor IS 256-byte aligned (or at least 16-byte aligned for float tensors). base+225 = base + 225*4 = base + 900. Since base is 256-byte aligned, base+900 mod 16 = (0 + 900) mod 16 = 4. Not 16-byte aligned! So for H*W=225, float4 loads are misaligned for every odd ic (and some even ic too). This could cause issues or performance degradation. To handle this safely, I'll just use scalar loads for now. We can add an alignment check and use float4 when aligned. Actually, on modern GPUs (sm_80+), misaligned 16-byte loads are handled efficiently via hardware. It might be 2 transactions instead of 1, but still OK. But let's be safe and not use float4 unless we know alignment. Now, for weights, consecutive threads with the same `lane` but different `oc_group` access different weight rows. If we iterate over `ic`, they access `weight[oc_start + k) * C_in + ic]` for different `oc_start`. These are NOT consecutive, but they are strided by `C_in`. For large `C_in`, they'll be in different cache lines but all accessed within a short time. If `C_in` is small (e.g., 16), weight[oc1*16 + ic] and weight[oc2*16 + ic] might be in the same or adjacent cache lines. OK let me write the code now. I'll be careful with the CUDA-PyTorch interface. I also need to handle the `load_inline` compilation. The module will be compiled at import time, so if there are compilation errors, we'll catch them with `compile_kernel`. Let me write a helper in Python: ```python def call_conv1x1_bn(input, weight, bn, act=0): # weight: [C_out, C_in, 1, 1] # reshape to [C_out, C_in] w = weight.view(weight.size(0), weight.size(1)).contiguous() return custom_ops.conv1x1_bn_cuda( input.contiguous(), w, bn.weight, bn.bias, bn.running_mean, bn.running_var, act ) ``` Wait, `bn.eps` is also needed. It's a scalar. We can pass it as a float. Similarly for depthwise: ```python def call_depthwise3x3_bn(input, weight, bn, stride, act=0): # weight: [C, 1, 3, 3] w = weight.view(weight.size(0), 9).contiguous() return custom_ops.depthwise3x3_bn_cuda( input.contiguous(), w, bn.weight, bn.bias, bn.running_mean, bn.running_var, stride, bn.eps, act ) ``` And for initial conv3x3: ```python def call_conv3x3_bn(input, weight, bn, stride, padding, act=0): return custom_ops.conv3x3_bn_cuda( input.contiguous(), weight, bn.weight, bn.bias, bn.running_mean, bn.running_var, stride, padding, bn.eps, act ) ``` For the classifier: ```python def call_avgpool_linear(input, fc): # input: [B, C, H, W] return custom_ops.avgpool_linear_cuda( input.contiguous(), fc.weight, fc.bias ) ``` Now I need to handle the fact that PyTorch `nn.Conv2d` weights might be on CPU during init but on GPU during forward. The CUDA kernels will be called when the model is on GPU. Let me also make sure to handle `bn.eps` correctly. In PyTorch, it's typically `1e-5` by default. One more thing: I need to include the correct headers and use `__restrict__` for pointer arguments to help the compiler optimize. I'll also add `__launch_bounds__` if needed, but I'll skip it for now. OK, I think I'm ready to write the code. Let me make a complete version and compile it.
